  • Smart Motorway Stocktake (2020/2022): The evidence review that effectively paused the All Lane Running (ALR) rollout. Changed the architectural direction from 'Capacity at low cost' to 'Safety retrofit regardless of cost'.
  • Technical Quality Score (0-100): Quantified metric derived from sub-criteria: Key Personnel (20%), Methodology (30%), Programme/Logic Links (15%), and Innovation/Lean delivery (5%). A score below 60 typically triggers a 'fail' condition regardless of price, safeguarding SRN resilience.
  • Collaborative Performance Framework (CPF) Scorecard: The primary metric for judging suppliers. Tracks KRA (Key Result Areas) across Safety (Home Safe and Well), Customer, and Delivery. NOTE: High scores here often mask underlying operational tensions regarding claim settlements and Compensation Events (CEs).
  • TOMs NT1 (Local Apprenticeships): Metric measuring number of Full Time Equivalent (FTE) local apprentices hired. A difficult metric for schemes like Smart Motorway Alliance (SMA) retrofits where work is sporadic and mobile, making long-term apprenticeships hard to sustain.
  • TOMs NT19 (Spend with Local SMEs): Monetary value of spend with local Small to Medium Enterprises. This creates a perverse incentive where Tier 1s may break large packages into inefficient smaller chunks to hit this KPI, potentially risking programme P50 availability.
  • Disallowed Cost Identification: The specific evaluation of costs that NH refuses to pay under NEC4 (e.g., costs incurred due to defects, rework, or lack of substantiation). This is a high-conflict area where commercial auditors actively reject portions of the 'Application for Payment' submitted by the Delivery Integration Partners (DIPs).
  • GG 104 Safety Case / Safety Risk Assessment: The formal output documenting that safety risks have been reduced to ALARP. For Smart Motorways, this is the critical artifact reviewed by the ORR. It is often treated as a 'shelf-ware' document by delivery teams rather than a living operational tool, creating a dangerous disconnect.
  • Scope 1 & 2 Emissions KPI (tCO2e): Key Performance Indicator for corporate emissions. Heavily weighted towards electricity consumption for tunnel lighting/ventilation and the Traffic Officer fleet. Currently red-rated due to delays in depot EV charging infrastructure rollout.
  • Lost Time Injury Frequency Rate (LTIFR): Key metric reported to the ORR. While currently stable, it often masks 'walking wounded' or stress-related absence which doesn't get categorized as a 'site injury'.
  • Sickness Absence Rate (Mental Health): Rising metric within the Customer Service Group. correlates strongly with Smart Motorway scrutiny and increased public aggression towards roadside staff.
  • Smart Motorways Evidence Stocktake: The critical post-mortem analysis of ALR (All Lane Running) safety data. This assessment effectively killed new Smart Motorway schemes and pivoted the entire architecture toward the ERA (Emergency Refuge Area) Retrofit Programme.
The critical post-mortem of All Lane Running (ALR) safety.
Relied heavily on comparative safety data from international networks to argue that ALR was statistically safer than conventional dual carriageways, despite public perception to the contrary.
The critical DfT outcome following the Transport Select Committee inquiry.
It froze ALR (All Lane Running) rollout and diverted funding toward SVD (Stopped Vehicle Detection).
This effectively de-funded several planned CAV expansion corridors to pay for immediate retrofit of radar SVD on existing M1 and M6 schemes.
The critical DfT analysis that effectively paused the rollout of new ALR schemes.
It forces a retrofit architecture (SVD, more ERAs) onto existing schemes, diverting capital from digital transformation initiatives.
The critical post-hoc assessment mandated by the Transport Select Committee.
It fundamentally altered the architecture of ALR schemes, forcing the retrofit of SVD technology and pausing the rollout of new ALR schemes pending safety data verification.
  • P50 Skid Resistance Metric: A critical safety KPI derived from SCANNER surveys. If P50 drops below investigatory levels, we have a statutory duty to intervene. This metric drives the surfacing renewal programme more than actual structural need, creating a 'worst-first' reactive maintenance culture.
  • KPI: P50 Journey Time Reliability: The primary customer promise in RIS2. Currently trending red due to the confluence of roadworks (A428, A66) and increased incident clearance times on Smart Motorways.
The metric measuring the planning time index.
Heavily influenced by long-term roadworks (WorkPackages).
The tension: Operations wants to lift roadworks to improve P50; Capital Delivery keeps them on to minimize mobilization costs.
The primary performance metric monitored by the ORR.
It measures the predictability of journey times.
Operationally, this metric drives perverse incentives—Regional Directors may prioritize keeping lanes open over necessary maintenance interventions to protect their P50 score, pushing asset degradation into the next RIS period.
Key KPI for the ORR and DfT.
Measures the consistency of journey times.
Operationally, this metric drives the focus on rapid incident clearance over long-term asset health, creating a perverse incentive to patch roads quickly rather than fix them properly.
Key RIS2 KPI measuring the 50th percentile of journey times.
Operational trade-off: Meeting this target encourages keeping lanes open during peak hours, which directly conflicts with the need to close lanes for essential defect rectification, leading to a deterioration of pavement condition (measured by TRACS).
Key performance indicator for RIS2.
There is a perverse incentive here: Closing a lane (Red X) for safety lowers the P50 score.
RCC operators must balance 'Keeping traffic moving' (Performance) vs 'Closing lanes for potential hazards' (Safety/Governance).
  • Defra Biodiversity Metric 4.0 Calculation: The specific spreadsheet-based calculation mechanism required to quantify 'Biodiversity Units'. Used on the A428 Black Cat and A66 Northern Trans-Pennine schemes. It converts UKHab survey data into unit values based on distinctiveness, condition, and strategic significance. A negative score stops the gateway process.
  • Skid Resistance (Scrim Coefficient): Safety critical data layer derived from SCANNER surveys. Areas falling below investigatory levels trigger CS 228 operational constraints (speed limits) until resurfacing occurs.
  • ADMM Data Quality Validation Score: Key Performance Indicator (KPI) measuring the percentage of asset data submitted by contractors that complies with ADMM syntax. Schemes frequently fail the initial P50 validation, leading to 'conditional acceptance' where we take ownership of the road but inherit dirty data, creating a long-tail liability for the regional maintenance teams.
  • Operational Metric: KPI P50 (Incident Clearance): Key Performance Indicator: Percentage of incidents cleared within 1 hour. Drives perverse behavior in RCCs, where operators may close lanes prematurely to 'secure the scene' and stop the clock, rather than attempting to keep traffic flowing, prioritizing the metric over journey time reliability.
  • Operational Compliance Verification (Inspection Body): External audit performed by an independent inspection body every 6 years. If the 'Administrative Authority' (DfT) does not sign off on this report based on TSO advice, the tunnel cannot legally operate. This is the 'nuclear option' in the governance structure that forces funding release for M&E assets over general carriageway defects.
  • GG 104 Safety Risk Assessment (SRA): The mandatory quantified risk assessment used to justify why legacy tunnels cannot meet modern CD 352 standards. It provides the legal cover for operating sub-standard geometry or aging systems by proving the risk is ALARP. A critical document for the Technical Assurance Authority (TAA).
The overarching safety risk standard.
The Principal Designer must prove that the design reduces risk to 'As Low As Reasonably Practicable' (ALARP).
Tensions arise when CDM 'elimination of hazard' conflicts with environmental constraints (e.g., noise barriers limiting visibility) on schemes like Lower Thames Crossing.
  • KPI: Skid Resistance (CS 228): Safety-critical metric derived from SCRIM surveys. Failure to meet investigatory levels defined in CS 228 triggers mandatory speed restrictions or lane closures, immediately impacting the P50 Availability metric. A tight coupling between asset condition and operational performance.
  • Supply Chain Hardware Trojan Risk: The threat of compromised microcode within the RSU communication modules (e.g., Quectel or Cinterion chips) introduced during manufacturing. We lack the deep-packet inspection capability at the edge to detect beaconing behaviour from compromised operational technology before it hits the National Roads Telecommunications Services (NRTS) network.
  • Operational Technology (OT) Cyber Security Capability Gap: Critical shortage of internal resource capable of securing roadside edge computing and V2X infrastructure against hostile state vectors. Current Traffic Officer Service (TOS) training covers physical safety (cones/tarmac) but omits digital asset protection. This creates a vulnerability surface on the A66 Northern Trans-Pennine digital twin pilot and threatens compliance with the NIS Directive.
  • Mode Confusion & Handover Risk: Operational assessment of the danger when drivers over-rely on ADAS (e.g., Lane Keep Assist) or misunderstand the Operational Design Domain (ODD) of current vehicle tech. This is the 'valley of death' for Level 3 autonomy. We are seeing this live on the M25 and M1 where drivers disengage, assuming the car/road is doing more than it can. A major tension point between vehicle marketing and actual infrastructure capability.
  • FTE vs Contractor Dependency Ratio: Metric tracking the balance between permanent staff and 'interim' supply chain resources (Tier 1 secondees). Highlights the perverse incentive where headcount caps force directorates to use CapEx budgets to hire expensive contractors for RIS3 planning (A66 Northern Trans-Pennine) to bypass Civil Service recruitment freezes.
  • Strategic Engineering Capability Attrition Rate: Specific measure of 'regrettable loss' in niche technical domains (ITS, bridge engineering, tunnel safety). We are currently bleeding talent to the supply chain (Amey, Balfour Beatty) who pay market rates that Civil Service pay bands cannot match, threatening our ability to act as an 'Intelligent Client' for DMRB governance.
  • EDI Senior Role Representation (2025 Targets): Tracks diversity specifically at Band 8 and SES (Senior Executive Service) levels against the 2025 Strategy. Currently shows a lag in operational leadership roles compared to corporate functions, creating a cultural disconnect between 'The Office' (The Cube/Bridge House) and the 'Outstations'.
  • Civil Service People Survey Engagement Index: The annual engagement score. Reveals significant variance between Major Projects Delivery (high engagement, clear mission on schemes like A303 Stonehenge) and Operations (lower engagement due to pay freezes and shift pressures). This metric is a lead indicator for union activity and potential industrial action.
  • ORR License Compliance Assessment: The periodic legal health-check determining if National Highways is meeting its conditions under the Road Investment Strategy (RIS). Failure here risks regulatory enforcement action from the Office of Rail and Road (ORR), specifically regarding efficiency targets and safety performance (KPI 1).
  • ICO Decision Notices (Overturn Risk): The metric of failure for the Information Rights team. A high overturn rate by the Information Commissioner's Office on our redactions (specifically regarding environmental impact assessments for DCO applications) signals systemic non-compliance and reputational damage.
  • Accident Frequency Rate (AFR): Key KPI reported to ORR and DfT. Aggregates workforce injuries (NH and Supply Chain). Heavily influenced by large capital schemes (RIS2 delivery) and often lags behind leading indicators like Safety Inspection scores.
  • Occupational Health Anxiety/Stress Index: Internal metric tracking mental health-related absence. Currently trending negatively in Customer Service Division due to increased abuse from public regarding Smart Motorway reliability and delays.
  • Data Science vs. Civil Engineering Cultural Friction: Severe organizational tension. Traditional Asset Delivery directorates prioritize physical condition metrics (LARS, SCRIM) over predictive data analytics. This results in data silos where 'Smart' motorway radar data (stopped vehicle detection) is not effectively integrated with long-term asset degradation models, hindering the transition to predictive maintenance envisioned in RIS3.
  • Capital Programme Variance Analysis: The monthly 'truth telling' exercise. We compare actual spend (Work Done) against the baseline. Current operational reality: Inflation is causing significant variance on schemes like Lower Thames Crossing, forcing 'virement' (transfers) from Renewals budgets, degrading long-term asset health (P50 metrics).
  • KPI: Pavement Surface Condition (SAMI/SCANNER): Data derived from TRACS/SCANNER surveys. Used to justify maintenance funding. There is a lag of up to 18 months between survey and intervention, meaning the 'Red' condition reported to DfT is often already a pothole by the time funding arrives.
  • Residual Safety Risk Profile: The aggregate level of risk (probability x severity of collisions) remaining on the network after a scheme opens with approved departures. This metric directly impacts the P50 Network Availability and KSI (Killed or Seriously Injured) targets monitored by the ORR.
  • ORR Annual Assessment of Safety and Performance: The Office of Rail and Road's regulatory review. Their scrutiny of Smart Motorway safety data (stopped vehicle detection times) directly dictates operational priorities, often overriding internal strategic roadmaps to force immediate remedial works.
  • Smart Motorway Stocktake Evidence Assessment: A pivotal, ongoing assessment of safety data (stopped vehicle detection rates, response times) following the Transport Select Committee inquiry. This assessment directly dictates the operational risk profile for All Lane Running (ALR) sections like the M25 J23-27.
  • Asset Condition Score (LARS/SCANNER): Quantitative assessment of road surface condition derived from SCANNER surveys and stored in LARS (Lane Administration & Registration System). A declining score acts as a leading indicator for 'Safety Risk' and 'Service Availability Risk', triggering funding release from the Renewals budget.
  • P50 Pavement Condition Metric: Key KPI reported to the ORR. Measures the percentage of road surface requiring investigation. Operations teams often game this metric by prioritizing cheap surface dressing on 'Amber' condition roads rather than expensive deep reconstruction on 'Red' roads, due to annual budget cycles preventing multi-year asset strategies.
  • ORR RIS2 Performance Assessment: The external regulator's 'report card'. specifically targeting the KPI for 'P50 Network Availability'. Recent interventions regarding the validity of 'Technology Availability' metrics (excluding loop faults) have forced a re-architecture of how availability data is aggregated in the Business Intelligence layer.
  • Supply Chain Capacity & Inflation Analysis: Assessment of the construction market's ability to deliver NSIPs (Nationally Significant Infrastructure Projects) like the A428 Black Cat to Caxton Gibbet. High inflation impacts the 'Value for Money' cases for RIS3, forcing de-scoping of non-critical elements.
  • Geotechnical Asset Risk Profile (A66/M62): Critical evaluation of slope stability under saturation conditions. Focuses on peat slide risk on the A66 Northern Trans-Pennine route and chalk dissolution risks on the M25 J6-7. Utilizes GDMS (Geotechnical Data Management System) data, though relies heavily on legacy paper records for pre-1980 assets, creating a 'blind spot' in resilience planning.
  • Asset Condition Assessment (LARS/HAPMS): The ground truth of the network. Data from TRACS and SCANNER surveys fed into HAPMS/PAMS. When LARS (Lane Availability Reporting System) shows degradation, it triggers a statutory duty to intervene, forcing the Investment Decision Committee to divert funds from 'Shiny' enhancements (RIS) to 'Dull' maintenance (Renewals), creating friction with political promises.
  • Smart Motorway Stocktake 2020: The pivotal evidence review. It concluded ALR is statistically safer but public perception is broken. This assessment forced the cancellation of new ALR schemes in RIS3 planning.
The seminal event that shifted the architectural focus from 'Capacity at all costs' to 'Safety evidence first'.
It mandated the rollout of SVD and paused all new ALR schemes (M3 J9-14, etc.) until 5 years of safety data is proven.
  • Killed or Seriously Injured (KSI) Metric: The paramount safety KPI monitored by DfT and ORR; the 2040 'Zero Harm' goal drives investment in costly central barrier replacements but conflicts with the budget cuts in routine maintenance.
The ultimate safety metric (Target: Zero Harm by 2040).
Operational reality: Statistical noise in KSI data makes short-term impact of interventions (like SVD) hard to prove to ORR.
  • Road Surface Skid Resistance (SCRIM/GripTester): Critical safety metric. Deteriorating scores on the strategic network are forcing reduced speed limits as a mitigation, masking the underlying funding gap for pavement renewals in the tail end of RIS2.
  • KPI: Killed or Seriously Injured (KSI): The ultimate safety metric (Target: Zero Harm). Lagging indicator. Recent spikes on Smart Motorways have driven the architectural pivot back towards physical infrastructure (more ERAs) rather than pure technology solutions.
The headline safety metric.
The 'Vision Zero' goal targets this.
The operational reality is that KSI stats often drive reactive safety schemes (e.g., barrier upgrades) at specific 'blackspots' rather than systemic network-wide treatments.
The primary lagging safety indicator.
Reporting is delayed by months due to the CRASH validation process with Police forces, making it useless for real-time operational steering at NTOC.
  • Corporate Risk: Supply Chain Inflation: Risk that RIS2/RIS3 committed schemes (A66, A46) become unaffordable due to construction inflation. This is forcing 'value engineering' (de-scoping) which often removes operational technology (CCTV density) to save capex, increasing long-term opex risk.
  • Smart Motorway Stocktake Evidence: The watershed assessment that altered operational architecture. It highlighted data gaps in stopped vehicle detection and forced a shift from 'capacity first' to 'safety assurance first', imposing retroactive constraints on ALR (All Lane Running) sections.
  • Structure Condition (BCI): Bridge Condition Index. Measures the degradation of structures. Many 1970s structures (M5 Oldbury, M6 Bromford) are deteriorating faster than funding allows for repair. This creates a 'hidden' operational risk where weight restrictions or lane closures may be imposed suddenly on strategic corridors.
  • RIS2 Efficiency & Inflation Review: ORR-led assessment of National Highways' ability to deliver the £27.4bn RIS2 capital programme. It highlights a massive trade-off: attempting to maintain the 'Road Period Efficiency' KPI while raw material costs for bitumen and steel have escalated, putting the A66 scheme's Benefit-Cost Ratio (BCR) at risk.
  • Cyber Security Maturity Assessment (NIS Directive): Compliance audit against the NIS Directive for Critical National Infrastructure (CNI). It reveals a high risk in 'Roadside OT'—specifically older CCTV and Message Signs in the South East region that are difficult to patch, creating a tension between operational uptime and security patching windows.
  • Gateway 3 (Investment Decision) Review: The critical 'Go/No-Go' point where the Full Business Case (FBC) is approved. This is the point of maximum friction. The tension lies between the fixed price needed for approval and the volatile construction inflation indices (Tender Price Index). Passing this gateway often requires 'Value Engineering' (scope reduction) which can compromise the original user benefits defined in the RIS2 commitment.
  • Smart Motorway Safety Evidence Stocktake 2020: The watershed moment for NH operations. Forced the admission that ALR 'safety' relied heavily on theoretical stats vs. public perception. Directly triggered the pause on new ALR schemes and mandated the SVD retrofit program.
  • Asset Resilience Risk Assessment (DMRB GG 104): Evaluation of safety risk using the ALARP (As Low As Reasonably Practicable) principle per GG 104. Currently flagging critical risks in drainage and geotechnical assets due to climate change. This assessment often reveals the funding gap between RIS2 distinct enhancements and the maintenance budget reality, impacting P50 Network Availability metrics.
  • National Road User Satisfaction Survey (NRUSS) Metrics: The official scorecard. It's heavily weighted by journey time and signage clarity. The 'Ageing Population' demographic consistently scores us lower on 'feeling safe', which directly impacts the ORR performance assessment.
  • KPI: P50 Network Availability: Primary RIS2/RIS3 performance metric monitored by ORR. Measures percentage of lanes available to traffic. Perverse incentive: Encourages delaying critical maintenance (renewal works) to keep lanes open, increasing long-term asset vulnerability and risk of catastrophic failure (e.g., concrete spalling).
Percentage of the network available to traffic.
Heavily impacted by roadworks.
This metric incentivizes 'lift and shift' maintenance (night work) which costs 30% more than 24-hour working, creating a tension with the Efficiency KPI.
Key performance metric measuring the average delay experienced by road users.
Currently trending Red/Amber due to high volume of roadworks (A428, M6) and incident clearance times.
Heavily influenced by the availability of lanes (LARS data).
The primary operational metric reported to ORR.
It measures the proportion of the network open to traffic.
It incentivises Operations to delay maintenance interventions (Roadspace Booking) to keep the score high, which ironically leads to worse asset condition (potholes) in the long run.
The primary operational metric for RIS2.
Measures the percentage of the network free from roadworks/incidents.
This creates a perverse incentive to delay necessary renewal works (pavement failures) to keep P50 high, pushing the 'asset maintenance bow wave' into RIS3.
The measure of the percentage of the network available to traffic.
This metric drives the scheduling of roadworks (WorkPackages).
It incentivizes delaying maintenance to night-time only, which extends project duration and cost, conflicting with the 'Efficiency' constraint.
The percentage of the SRN available to traffic, penalised by lane closures.
This metric creates perverse incentives: Asset delivery teams are pressured to delay necessary pavement renewals (to keep availability high) until surface failure becomes critical, forcing emergency repairs that are more disruptive.

It incentivizes keeping lanes open.

  • SRN Radio Coverage Assessment (A66/A69): Operational reality check: Significant 'not-spots' exist on the Strategic Road Network, particularly the A66 and A69. In these zones, Traffic Officers operate without radio cover, relying on lone-worker safety protocols that are procedural rather than technological. This is a known risk in the operational safety case.
  • KPI: Network Availability (RIS2 Performance Framework): The statutory metric (target >97%) monitored by the ORR. Calculation: Total Lane-Kilometre-Minutes available / Total Lane-Kilometre-Minutes theoretical max. This metric drives perversity in operational behavior, forcing Maintenance & Response contracts to prioritize 'availability' over 'grouping of works', leading to inefficient, repeated visits to the same asset to avoid long-duration closures.
  • TRACS and SCRIM Data Analysis: Analysis of data from TRACS (TRAffic-speed Condition Surveys) and SCRIM (Sideway-force Coefficient Routine Investigation Machine) to determine the structural and surface health of the carriageway.
  • Strategic Road Network Customer Satisfaction Score: Key Performance Indicator (KPI) measuring driver satisfaction, derived from Transport Focus surveys, which influences regulatory grading by the ORR.

ARCHIMATE:BUSINESSACTOR (114 components):
--------------------------------------
  • DfT Roads Strategy Division (RSD): The specific DfT unit acting as the Client for the Road Investment Strategy (RIS). They hold the ultimate veto on Programme Business Cases (PBC) for Tier 1 schemes like Lower Thames Crossing and A66 Northern Trans-Pennine. They do not just provide oversight; they control the 'Statements of Funds Available' (SoFA) release triggers.
  • HM Treasury Infrastructure and Projects Authority (IPA): The external assurance body conducting 'Gate Reviews' (0-5). A 'Red' or 'Amber/Red' rating from IPA on a major scheme like the A428 Black Cat to Caxton Gibbet effectively freezes funding release, regardless of NH's internal readiness or contractor mobilization.
  • National Highways Investment Committee (IC): The internal gatekeeper for Tier 2 (£100m-£500m) and Tier 3 (<£100m) investments. Theoretically holds the kill switch, but rarely cancels schemes explicitly named in the Road Investment Strategy (RIS) due to ORR regulatory penalties for missed commitments.
  • Operations Directorate (National Network): The entity holding the 'License to Operate'. They are the duty holders for the SRN. They inherit assets from Major Projects often without the requisite OpEx uplift to maintain them. They run the RCCs and manage the Traffic Officer Service.
The end-users of the H&S File.
They maintain the asset post-delivery.
They are currently struggling with the 'digital gap'—receiving complex smart motorway assets without the requisite digital twins or updated maintenance manuals in the asset management system.
  • Major Projects Directorate (Complex Infrastructure): Responsible for delivering Tier 1 schemes (A428, A66, LTC). They operate under immense pressure to hit 'Start of Works' dates to satisfy DfT RIS commitments, sometimes leading to 'value engineering' that strips out operational resilience features to stay within budget, passing technical debt to Operations.
Delivery arm responsible for RIS2/RIS3 Tier 1 schemes (A303 Stonehenge, A66 Northern Trans-Pennine).

Operates in tension with Major Projects, often inheriting 'value-engineered' assets that are expensive to maintain.

Operational staff responsible for signal setting and incident management.
Tension: Must balance ORR mandates for 'verified' incidents before setting Red X against the immediate safety risk of stalled vehicles in live lanes on Smart Motorways.
  • West Midlands RCC Operator (Quinton): The human 'swivel-chair' interface responsible for validating incoming alerts before setting 'Red X' signals (TSRGD compliance). Operational constraint: Cognitive load is maxed out during peak flows; operators must visually verify SVD alerts via CCTV before signal setting to avoid litigation, creating a critical dependency on CCTV P50 availability.
  • RCC Godstone Control Room Operator: Operational staff managing the M25 South East quadrant. Responsible for interpreting SVD alerts. High cognitive load due to 'ghost' alerts. They hold the specific delegation to set Red X signals under TSRGD requirements.
Operational staff responsible for the M25/A3 corridor.
Must cognitively switch between standard motorway traffic management and specific tunnel safety protocols defined in the Local Operating Agreement.
  • On-Road Traffic Officer Service (TOS): Frontline responders patrolling specific beats (e.g., M6 J13-J15). Operational tension exists here: they are dual-tasked with 'Customer Service' and 'Safety Critical Response,' often under-resourced during peak flows leading to missed KPIs.
  • RCC Quinton (West Midlands Control Centre): Operational hub managing the M6 Smart Motorway corridor. Houses the specific desk dispatchers who own the decision rights for TO deployment. Friction point: Tensions often arise between RCC Quinton's tactical lane closure needs and NTOC's strategic requirement to keep the M6 flowing for national freight logistics.
  • Traffic Officer Service (TOS) Operations: The operational workforce comprising ~1,900 uniformed staff responsible for incident management on the SRN. Currently facing a critical trade-off: delivering the '10-minute response time' commitment for Stopped Vehicle Detection (SVD) alerts on All Lane Running (ALR) sections while managing a 12-15% regional attrition rate (particularly in South East regions like Godstone). Operational capacity is heavily constrained by the 'Smart Motorway Stocktake' commitments vs. flat operational expenditure (OpEx) settlements in RIS2/RIS3.
  • NTOC (National Traffic Operations Centre) Quinton Team: The strategic command hub responsible for the Strategic Road Network (SRN) overview. They manage the 'Strategic diversion routes' when major incidents close links like the M62 Trans-Pennine. They are distinct from Regional Control Centres (RCCs) but depend entirely on the same Common Operational Picture (COP) provided by NTIS. If NTIS latency exceeds 2 minutes, their ability to coordinate cross-regional diversions collapses.
  • Road User (Commercial & Freight): Logistics operators heavily reliant on journey time reliability (BIT - Budgeted Impact Time). They bypass official VMS information in favour of third-party telematics which often route HGVs onto inappropriate local roads when the SRN is congested, causing friction with Local Highway Authorities.
  • Telent (NRTS Operator): Operates the fibre/copper roadside network. Critical dependency for all Smart Motorway signals. Maintenance windows for NRTS often conflict with operational needs; a fibre cut on the M62 can blind the Wakefield RCC, forcing a reversion to manual patrols and 60mph fixed limits.
  • SDF Community (Scheme Delivery Framework): The supply chain ecosystem delivering renewals. The architectural tension lies in the commercial model: incentives for speed sometimes conflict with data quality requirements (ADMM) at handover, leaving NH with 'orphan assets' in the database.
  • Regional Engineering Stewards: Senior engineers in Operations directorates (e.g., East, South West) who hold the delegated authority to sign off technical standards deviations (Departures). They often reject centrally modeled renewal recommendations based on 'local knowledge', creating a disconnect between the National Asset Needs plan and actual delivery.
  • Operations Directorate (OD): The asset receiver. Responsible for maintenance (Regionally via ADs) and traffic management (NTOC/RCCs). They bear the long-term risk of poor data; if pavement depths are unrecorded in HAPMS, they cannot accurately forecast TWP (Treatment Works Programme) budgets.
The 'poor relation' regarding funding but holds the statutory duty for network availability.
Manages RCCs, Traffic Officers, and maintenance via ASC/AD contracts.
Inherits high-tech assets from MP (like smart motorways) without proportional OPEX uplifts for software maintenance.
The custodian of the SRN responsible for real-time traffic management (Traffic Officers), maintenance, and renewals; currently struggling with aging assets and the resource drain of the Smart Motorway Safety Retrofit programme diverting funds from pavement renewals.
The primary First Line actor responsible for the Strategic Road Network (SRN) real-time performance.
manages the Traffic Officer Service, RCCs (e.g., Godstone, Quinton), and maintains asset availability (P50 metrics).
They own the immediate safety risk during incidents.
The National Highways directorate responsible for the day-to-day operation and maintenance of the SRN, managing the Maintenance and Response (M&R) contracts and Regional Operations Centres (ROCs).
  • Regional Engineering & Asset Teams (OD/SES): The decision-makers in the regions (e.g., South East, Midlands) who validate model outputs. They frequently override dTIMS recommendations based on local intelligence (e.g., knowing a specific batch of aggregate on the A14 is polishing faster than predicted).   • Regional Control Centre (RCC): Tactical command nodes (e.g., RCC Godstone, RCC Wakefield). Responsible for setting VMS and dispatching Traffic Officers. Tension: Disconnect between RCC tactical views (CCTV based) and National Command strategic views (NTIS data based).
The human loop in the 'automated' Smart Motorway system.
They manually verify SVD alerts because the false positive rate on radar detection is too high to automate Red X signal setting without visual confirmation.
  • Regional Control Centre (RCC) Operators: The humans in the loop at Godstone, South Mimms, Wakefield, etc. They stare at HART screens and CCTV. They are the primary integration point between faulty sensor data (MIDAS) and safety-critical decisions (Setting Red X). Cognitive load is a major concern.

Specialist National Highways delivery teams responsible for high-value, high-risk NSIPs such as the Lower Thames Crossing and A303 Stonehenge.
  • SMP Alliance (Smart Motorways Programme): Consortium delivering ALR retrofits and safety enhancements. Pivot from new capacity delivery to safety retrofit (SVD, additional ERAs) following the 2022 'Pause' and ORR Evidence Stocktake. Politically toxic but operationally essential for capacity.
  • Planning Inspectorate (PINS): Executive agency acting as the Examining Authority. They hold the 'gatekeeper' power for Acceptance and write the recommendation report. They utilize the PINS Infrastructure Planning Portal for all examination library documentation.

They do not care about NH's internal deadlines; they care about due process.
They hold the power to recommend refusal of Development Consent if consultation was flawed.

Incentivised primarily on Capital Efficiency and Schedule Performance Index (SPI), often detached from long-term asset maintainability.

Measured on P50 Network Availability and customer satisfaction, often lacking budget to remediate value-engineered defects.
  • Lower Thames Crossing Delivery Team: The specific MPDD (Major Projects Delivery Directorate) unit responsible for the largest DCO application in UK history. Special Purpose Vehicle for the £9bn+ scheme.

  • ORR (Office of Rail and Road): The monitor holding NH to account on Efficiency and Safety KPIs. Their scrutiny on 'Smart Motorways' (ALR) safety following the Evidence Stocktake has created a risk-averse culture where innovations affecting the carriageway face extreme evidentiary burdens before approval.
  • Department for Transport (DfT) Roads Strategy Unit: Government body holding the purse strings for RIS2/RIS3. They demand P50 availability while simultaneously capping Opex, creating a structural deficit in asset renewal. Direct oversight of National Highways via the Framework Document.

  • Operations Directorate (Regional Control Centres): The 'customer' of the roadworks. RCCs (e.g., Godstone, Quinton) deny road space bookings if suppliers fail NOMS compliance. They must manage the SRN using systems like HART and CHARM, dealing with the operational reality of compromised geometry (e.g., lack of hard strips) long after the project team has disbanded.
  • Operational Technology Maintenance Contractor (RTMC Tech): Key Supplier category. Responsible for keeping MIDAS, CCTV, and HADECS operational. If they fail S LAs, the control room is blind. Their performance is measured differently (Maintenance & Response) compared to the capital delivery partners.
  • Project Commercial Director (A428 Black Cat): The individual accountable for commercial governance on the £1bn+ A428 scheme. Operates with a dual reporting line: functionally to the C&P Directorate but operationally to the Project Director, creating a tension between cost control (C&P) and schedule delivery (Project).
  • National Audit Office (NAO): External independent auditor that scrutinizes NH's delivery of the Road Investment Strategy. Their 'VfM studies' drive internal anxiety and trigger defensive documentation practices within the Complex Infrastructure Programme (e.g., Lower Thames Crossing).
  • Kier Highways (A66 Delivery Partner): Tier 1 Integration Partner for A66. Focused on earthworks and structures (Tier 2 management) while adhering to strict carbon reduction targets in the construction phase.
  • Keller Geotechnique (Tier 2 Specialist): Specialist ground engineering sub-contractor. Critical for the complex piling works required on the A66 upgrade. A breakdown here delays the entire critical path.
  • Design Joint Venture (Amey/Arup/WSP): External consultancies fulfilling the PD role for schemes like the A66. They must identify, eliminate, or control foreseeable risks. A critical friction point is their reliance on incomplete historical asset data from HAPMS/IAM IS to inform design risk assessments.
  • Major Projects (MP) Delivery Teams: The operational teams delivering NSIPs like Lower Thames Crossing and A66 Northern Trans-Pennine. They struggle with rigid ERP cost codes that don't match NEC4 contract structures.
  • Rijkswaterstaat (Netherlands): Primary European peer for National Highways regarding high-density traffic management and smart motorway logic. NH heavily benchmarked Dutch tunnel safety systems (Tunnelwet) during the design of the A303 Stonehenge and Lower Thames Crossing tunnels to mitigate risk exposure in the DCO process.
Dutch counterpart and primary benchmarking partner.
NH draws heavily on their 'Minder Hinder' (Less Nuisance) approach to roadworks and their advanced tunnel safety standards.
They operate the CHARM traffic management platform which NH is attempting to replicate.
  • Transport Scotland: Devolved Administration peer. Operational friction occurs at the A74(M)/M6 border (Gretna) regarding winter resilience standards and cross-border incident management. Different skid resistance policies create a patchwork compliance landscape for hauliers.
  • Austroads (Australia/NZ): Key knowledge source for asset management in extreme weather. As UK summers get hotter (impacting asphalt rutting/melting), NH SES teams are rapidly importing Australian pavement standards to update the MCHW (Manual of Contract Documents for Highway Works).
  • Department for Transport (DfT) Roads Minister: Ultimate funding authority holding the RIS2/RIS3 purse strings. Currently exerting extreme pressure on NH to demonstrate 'value for money' on the Lower Thames Crossing while simultaneously demanding Smart Motorway safety retrofits without additional budget provision.

ARCHIMATE:BUSINESSCOLLABORATION (19 components):
----------------------------------------------
  • Remuneration Committee: Determines Executive pay and bonus structures based on corporate KPIs (Safety, Customer Service, Delivery). A point of contention is how to weigh 'Network Availability' (P50 metrics) against 'Safety Performance' when operational decisions (like closing lanes for ERA retrofit) hurt availability scores.
  • Nominations Committee: Ensures the Board has the right mix of skills (engineering, digital, finance). Currently struggling to find sufficient digital expertise to oversee the massive 'Digital Roads' transformation and the shift from roadside technology to in-vehicle connectivity (V2X).
  • Investment Decision Committee (IDC): The primary battlefield for internal resource allocation. Chaired by the CEO/CFO; mediates the fierce tension between Major Projects (RIS delivery) and Operations (Asset Delivery). Often favours 'ribbon-cutting' capital schemes over unglamorous maintenance, leading to an asset renewal backlog.
  • Departures from Standards Board: The governance forum where technical safety requirements (SES) clash with delivery reality (MP). This is the 'shadow architecture' where GG 104 safety risk assessments are traded off against schedule delays. The source of significant technical debt on the SRN.
  • Audit and Risk Assurance Committee (ARAC): The 'teeth' of the Board. Chaired by a Non-Executive Director (NED). They review the 'Lines of Defence' assurance model. Currently heavily focused on the cyber risks of the transition from legacy NTIS to the cloud-native HART system, and the financial forecasting accuracy of major schemes like the A66 Northern Trans-Pennine project.
  • Safety Committee: Chaired by a NED with safety expertise. They monitor the 'Home Safe and Well' strategy. Operational friction exists here: they demand rigorous adherence to GG 104 (Safety Risk Assessment) for All Lane Running (ALR) schemes, which often clashes with the Major Projects delivery schedules. They review the efficacy of Stopped Vehicle Detection (SVD) performance in RCCs.
  • Investment Committee: Delegated authority to approve capital expenditure between £100m and £500m (above this goes to DfT/Treasury). They control the SGAR (Stage Gate Assessment Review) outcomes. They are the ones who decide if the A428 Black Cat scheme proceeds to construction or gets held back due to budget constraints.
  • National Severe Weather Cell (Gold Command): Ad-hoc strategic decision body activated during Amber/Red warnings. Comprises Incident Management leads from NTOC, Regional Directors, and Supply Chain representatives. Tension exists here between keeping the Strategic Road Network (SRN) open (RIS KPI requirement) and safety closure mandates (GG 104 safety risk).
  • Complex Infrastructure Programme (CIP) Team: Cross-functional team handling high-risk schemes (A303, LTC). Bridges the gap between MPD delivery focus and General Counsel's risk aversion regarding DCO wording.
Cross-functional team managing Tier 1 schemes (A428, A66, LTC).
Heavily siloed from Operations, leading to 'throw it over the wall' handover issues where maintenance requirements for new assets (e.g., complex tunnel ventilation) are not fully costed in OPEX models.
  • CEDR Transnational Research Programme: Formal mechanism where NH pools resources with peers (Denmark, Netherlands, Germany) to research C-ITS (Cooperative Intelligent Transport Systems). This is a strategic workaround to conduct innovation despite RIS2 Efficiency constraints cutting internal R&D budgets.
  • Smart Motorways Programme (SMP) Alliance: Strategic (Tier 1) collaboration of 6 partners (Fluor, Jacobs, WSP, BMS, etc.) intended to deliver the ALR retrofit programme. Currently pivoting to 'safety retrofit' only following the government pause on new ALR schemes. Operates under a shared risk/reward model that is currently strained by scope volatility.
  • Regional Delivery Partnership (RDP) Supply Chain: The coalition of Tier 1 contractors (e.g., Balfour Beatty, Costain, Skanska) who actually deliver the work. They are squeezed between low-margin delivery pressures and the administrative burden of reporting TOMs data to National Highways.
  • Regional Control Centres (RCCs): Operational hubs (e.g., Godstone, Newton-le-Willows) executing tactical traffic management. Friction: Operators often mistrust automated algorithms from CHARM/NTIS, overriding them manually based on local CCTV observations.
  • Regional Delivery Partnership (RDP) Integration Team: The Tier 1 delivery vehicle for RIS2 schemes like the A428 and A66. Supposed to integrate design and build, but often suffers from 'throw it over the wall' behavior between early design (pre-DCO) and construction delivery. Heavily reliant on Tier 2 supply chains for actual plant/labour.
The delivery vehicle for schemes <£500m.
It forces collaboration between designers and contractors (e.g., Costain/Jacobs).
The friction point is the handover of data - the BIM models produced here rarely map cleanly into the NH asset management systems (IAM IS).
  • National Highways Data Science & AI Lab: Collaboration with supply chain partners to bridge the internal skills gap. Focuses on automating the analysis of SCANNER and TRACS pavement data to predict pothole formation (P50 metrics). Currently hindered by legacy data governance issues within the ADDS (Asset Data Definitions Structure).
  • Investment Programme Committee (IPC): The critical gatekeeper forum for capital approvals; often creates a bottleneck where operational urgencies clash with rigid 5-year RIS funding cycles and governance papers required for DfT investment decisions.
  • A428 Black Cat to Caxton Gibbet Delivery Team: Joint venture delivery team (Skanska/National Highways) executing the £1bn+ RIS2 scheme. Currently managing acute tension between aggressive construction milestones and the requirement to validate digital asset data (ADMM Part 2) prior to handover.
  • ORR (Office of Rail and Road) Holding to Account: The regulator enforcing RIS2/RIS3 efficiency and safety. They are sceptical of 'shiny' future mobility projects (MaaS/AMoD) if basic asset condition (skid resistance, drainage) deteriorates. This creates a governance tension where innovation funding is often raided to plug maintenance budget holes.
  • Smart Motorways Stocktake Taskforce: A specific cross-directorate body formed to address the intense scrutiny on ALR (All Lane Running) schemes. Generated specific lessons that forced retrofitting of Stopped Vehicle Detection (SVD) technology.

ARCHIMATE:BUSINESSEVENT (15 components):
--------------------------------------
  • Stage 0 Strategy (Strategic Outline Case) Review: Gateway review determining strategic alignment with RIS goals. High failure rate for regional schemes that fail to demonstrate BCR (Benefit-Cost Ratio) > 1.5 early on. Independent reviewers look for alignment with the Road to Net Zero strategy.
  • Stage 2 Option Selection (Outline Business Case) Review: Critical gateway where the 'Preferred Route Announcement' (PRA) is authorised. Historically a bottleneck where environmental constraints (biodiversity net gain) clash with engineering feasibility. Requires sign-off from the Chief Analyst regarding traffic modelling data.
  • Stage 3 Preliminary Design (Full Business Case) Review: The 'Point of No Return' investment decision. Requires a matured GG 104 safety file and locked target price. This is where the tension between the Supply Chain's inflation costs and the fixed RIS budget envelope is most acute.
  • Smart Motorway Evidence Stocktake (2020/2022): The pivotal regulatory event that fundamentally altered TOS operations. It mandated the retrofitting of additional Emergency Areas (ERAs) and forced the acceleration of Stopped Vehicle Detection (SVD). This event shifted the TOS focus from 'traffic flow' to 'live lane safety' almost exclusively.
  • Met Office National Severe Weather Warning (Amber/Red): External trigger event indicating high impact weather. Specific thresholds: Snow >5cm accumulation, Mean wind speeds >50mph (High Sided Vehicles) or >60mph (All traffic). Triggers automatic escalation to National Crisis Management Command structures.
  • Value Management (VM) Workshop: The operational 'battleground' where Asset Needs (what the data says) meets Commercial Reality (budget availability). Schemes are cut, deferred, or descoped here. Often results in 'holding treatments' (patching) being selected over necessary deep renewals, kicking the can down the road to RIS4.
  • Aquaplaning Risk Event: The safety realization of drainage failure. Triggers immediate control centre (RCC) intervention (speed limits, lane closures) and subsequent GG 104 safety risk assessments. The primary driver for priority renewals.
  • DCO Application Acceptance: The critical gate where PINS has 28 days to decide if the application (and the consultation behind it) meets standards. If this fails (as seen in early A303 attempts), the scheme is set back by months/years. This is the hard deadline driving the 'Consultation Report' production.
  • Clause 60.1 Compensation Event (Ground Conditions): The 'Unforeseen Physical Conditions' claim. Contractors on the A428 and A303 are leveraging this to claim time and money for soil toxicity or water table issues that 'an experienced contractor could not have foreseen', straining the contingency budget.
  • Red X Non-Compliance: A specific behavioural failure where drivers ignore lane closures. This is a proxy for the 'Behavioral' aspect of CAV acceptance. If users won't obey simple distinct signals (TSRGD mandated), the complex handover protocols required for Level 3/4 autonomy are operationally terrifying.
  • VRS Strike M6 J14: Specific trigger event: Heavy Goods Vehicle impacts concrete step barrier. Immediate operational crisis that triggers the friction between traffic flow restoration duties and asset integrity standards.
  • Unplanned Stationary Vehicle Incident (SVD): Detection of a stopped vehicle in a live lane on ALR (All Lane Running) sections (e.g., M1 J13-16, M25 J23-27). Critical input for the Smart Motorways Evidence Stocktake commitments. Triggers immediate safety protocol activation under GG 104 constraints.
  • Pavement Surface Failure (Heat Buckling): Specific incident type occurring during temperature extremes (>35°C), primarily affecting concrete bay sections (A14, M11). Requires immediate emergency traffic management (ETM) and unplanned surfacing interventions, impacting Network Availability (P50) metrics.
  • M1 J34 Operational Failure Incident: Representative of legacy ALR failures where a stopped vehicle was not detected by MIDAS, and CCTV coverage was obscured. Highlighted the gap between 'theoretical availability' of technology and actual operational detection capability.
  • IPV Strike / Incursion Event: A specific high-risk operational event where a live lane vehicle impacts a traffic management vehicle. Mandatory recording in HART. These events trigger immediate 'Safety Alert' workflows to the wider community.

ARCHIMATE:BUSINESSFUNCTION (33 components):
-----------------------------------------
  • Network Operations & Maintenance (Revenue Funded): The operational reality funded by a constrained ~£2bn annual revenue budget. Responsible for winter service, Traffic Officer response, and reactive repairs. Heavily reliant on the Asset Delivery model, currently struggling with inflation eroding purchasing power for basic materials like salt and bitumen.
  • SES Technical Authority & Assurance: The 'Governance Gatekeeper' within Safety, Engineering and Standards (SES). Responsible for owning the DMRB (Design Manual for Roads and Bridges). Currently struggling to update rigid standards (like GG 104) fast enough to accommodate agile digital innovations, creating a 'pace layering' mismatch with the Digital Roads strategy.
  • Major Infrastructure Delivery Procurement (RDP & DIP): Manages the Regional Delivery Partnership (RDP) and Delivery Integration Partners (DIP) frameworks. This function is currently strained by the hyper-inflation impact on RIS2/RIS3 baseline budgets, specifically for the A428 Black Cat and A66 Northern Trans-Pennine schemes. Accountability for design risk transfer under NEC4 Target Cost contracts remains a significant point of friction.
  • Operations & Maintenance Procurement (Asset Delivery): Responsible for procuring the supply chain that keeps the network running: winter services, pavement renewals, and vegetation management. The shift from Asset Support Contracts (ASC) to the in-house Asset Delivery (AD) model has shifted risk back to NH, exposing gaps in our internal capability to act as the 'intelligent client' for scheduling work packages.
  • Technology and Telecoms Procurement (NRTS & OT): Manages the National Roads Telecommunications Services (NRTS2) and operational technology purchasing. This is where legacy meets ambition; we are trying to buy 'Digital Roads' capability (V2X, Edge Computing) while still paying to maintain 20-year-old copper networks and aging MIDAS loops that are failing due to pavement degradation.
  • Professional Services Procurement (SPaTS 2): Manages the Specialist Professional and Technical Services (SPaTS 2) framework. This is how we buy brains—modelling, environmental consultancy, and legal advice for DCO applications. There is a perverse incentive here: consultants are paid by time, while NH seeks outcome-based speed, particularly for DCO approvals which are lagging.
  • Safety, Health and Wellbeing (SHW) Division: The central governance body attempting to standardize occupational health across the Strategic Road Network (SRN). Currently managing the tension between the 'Home Safe and Well' strategy and the intense delivery pressures of RIS2 close-out on schemes like the A428 Black Cat.
  • Commercial and Procurement (C&P) Assurance: The 'Second Line of Defence' responsible for verifying 'Defined Cost' across the RIS2 and RIS3 portfolio. This function creates operational friction with Major Projects delivery teams (e.g., A428, A66) by enforcing strict substantiation requirements on monthly Applications for Payment, often delaying cash flow to Tier 1 contractors to ensure Value for Money.
  • Human Resources & Organisational Development: The central function managing the NH workforce, currently under significant pressure to deliver RIS3 capability plans amidst public sector headcount caps. It struggles with the dual mandate of supporting corporate office staff (The Cube, Birmingham) and the operational 24/7 Traffic Officer Service across 7 regions. Heavily reliant on Oracle HCM.
  • General Counsel & Statutory Governance: The centralized legal authority ensuring National Highways operates within the License issued by the DfT. Unlike a standard corporate legal team, this function actively manages the tension between rapid RIS3 delivery and the rigid statutory constraints of the Highways Act 1980 and Planning Act 2008. They are the gatekeepers for Development Consent Orders (DCOs) and hold the ultimate risk ownership for NEC4 contract disputes.
  • NEC4 Contract Governance & Dispute Resolution: The function administering the complex commercial logic of NEC4 ECC contracts. It manages Compensation Events (CEs), Early Warning Notices (EWNs), and Pain/Gain share calculations. A key friction point is the interpretation of 'Disallowed Cost' under the Scheme of Construction Options, particularly regarding substantiation of supply chain defined costs.
  • Major Projects Directorate (MP): The capital delivery arm. They are incentivized to deliver schemes like A66 and Lower Thames Crossing on time/budget (CPI), often value-engineering out technology elements that Operations later desperately needs.
  • Operations Directorate: The revenue-funded arm managing the SRN. They inherit the assets built by MP. They are currently struggling with the 'Do More With Less' efficiency challenge while managing aging structures and the Smart Motorway legacy.
  • Provides corporate safety governance and improvement programmes: Provides safety policy, standards, and improvement oversight
  • Provides frontline operational safety execution: Provides incident response and safe network operations
  • Supplies real-time traffic management services: Provides control room monitoring and traffic management
  • Enables operational decision-making and traveller information: Provides network monitoring platforms and comms infrastructure
  • Ensures infrastructure condition is maintained: Provides inspection planning and lifecycle management
  • Enables delivery of contracted maintenance activity: Provides supplier procurement and contract governance
  • Executes infrastructure enhancement programmes: Provides construction and commissioning of improvements
  • Supplies evidence base for investment decisions: Provides modelling and scheme prioritisation outputs
  • Supplies journey information and public messaging: Provides updates, campaigns and customer communications
  • Feeds status and incident data into customer channels: Provides real-time operational network data
  • Supplies environmental monitoring and mitigation: Provides monitoring, reporting and mitigation programmes
  • Implements environmental controls in projects: Provides mitigation delivery within schemes
  • Oversees contract compliance and supplier performance: Provides contract monitoring and supplier assurance
  • Supplies governance reporting and controls: Provides reporting, cost control and compliance analytics
  • Supplies strategy and planning outputs to government: Provides RIS planning and strategic modelling
  • Ensures transparent reporting and accountability: Provides budget control and regulatory reporting
  • Sets strategic direction and performance expectations: Provides organisational governance and strategic oversight
  • Safety, Engineering and Standards (SES): The National Highways technical authority function responsible for setting DMRB standards, acting as the Technical Approval Authority (TAA), and ensuring the long-term safety and integrity of the SRN.
  • Structures Technical Authority (TAA): Specialized sub-function within SES or delegated regional engineering teams responsible for 'Technical Approval of Highway Structures' in accordance with DMRB CG 300.
  • Asset Management Operations: The core operational function responsible for the strategic planning, condition monitoring, and lifecycle engineering of physical assets (pavements, structures, technology) on the SRN.

ARCHIMATE:BUSINESSINTERACTION (5 components):
--------------------------------------------
  • Tactical Diversion Route Activation (Solid Black Shape): Re-routing SRN traffic onto Local Highway Authority (LHA) roads. Friction point: LHAs often resist activation due to lack of funding for resulting road degradation. Legacy issue: Some routes still require physical 'flap' sign turning by contractors.
  • Collaborative Performance Framework (CPF) Assessment: The monthly ritual where NH scores suppliers. In reality, scores are often inflated by delivery teams to maintain relationships, masking underlying performance issues until they become critical failures (e.g., delayed concrete testing data).
  • Transport Select Committee (TSC) Evidence Session: The high-stakes interaction where NH Executive Directors are held accountable by MPs. Requires extensive preparation by Public Affairs to explain variances in RIS2 delivery, specifically the pause on new All Lane Running (ALR) schemes and the retrofit of Emergency Areas (ERAs) on the M6 and M1. Failure here impacts future RIS3 funding settlements.
  • Commercial Settlement of Defects: The interaction between MP Commercial teams and Tier 1 contractors. Tensions arise when Ops identifies latent defects (skid resistance failure) post-12-month rectification period, forcing Ops to fund repairs from routine maintenance budgets.
  • Quarterly Performance & Risk Review (QPRR): The high-stakes meeting where Directorate risk registers are challenged by the Executive. Often a theater of negotiation where Safety, Engineering and Standards (SES) push for higher risk visibility while Major Projects attempts to downgrade risk ratings to maintain 'Green' delivery status.

ARCHIMATE:BUSINESSINTERFACE (2 components):
------------------------------------------
  • Single Point of Failure: National Roads Telecommunications Services (NRTS): The fiber/copper network connecting 30,000 roadside devices to RCCs. If NRTS goes down, the Smart Motorway signals go dark, and the road must be physically closed by Traffic Officers. A massive operational risk.
  • Section 278 Developer Contribution Process: The mechanism for 'Value Capture' where third-party developers fund SRN improvements (e.g., new junctions for logistics parks). Operational reality: A nightmare of conflicting incentives. Developers want minimum viable product for planning consent; NH Architecture requires GG 104 safety compliance and 60-year asset life, leading to prolonged technical approval cycles.

ARCHIMATE:BUSINESSOBJECT (89 components):
---------------------------------------
  • RIS2 License: The statutory instrument defining the Secretary of State's directions to NH. It includes legally binding conditions on efficiency (Conditions 5.23) which conflict with the DfT's frequent changes to scope (e.g., pausing the M3 J9 scheme), leaving NH accountable for efficiency targets they cannot operationally control.
  • HM Treasury Green Book (5 Case Model): The mandatory framework for all business cases. Specifically, the 'Economic Case' (BCR) often conflicts with the 'Strategic Case' (Levelling Up). This tension forces creative accounting on schemes like A66 to justify the Value for Money (VfM) when standard transport economics say 'do not build'.
  • Lower Thames Crossing DCO Application: The largest DCO (Development Consent Order) ever undertaken. It consumes vast amounts of SES and Major Projects bandwidth. The architectural trade-off is the sheer cost of the tunnel safety systems (ventilation, fire suppression) vs. the economic benefit model.
The Development Consent Order documentation for the LTC.
Represents a massive accumulation of statutory constraints that will bind Operations for 100 years.
The 'Operational Phase' requirements in the DCO are often drafted by planners, not operators, creating future compliance traps.
The massive Development Consent Order documentation for the LTC.
It has become a 'zombie' architectural object—consuming vast legal and planning resources while trapped in planning purgatory, distorting resource allocation away from smaller, deliverable renewal schemes.
A massive dataset representing the most advanced 'Digital Twin' model we have for a single scheme.
It represents a tension: it is highly detailed but incompatible with the national operational model used by legacy regional control centres.
Massive dataset required for the Development Consent Order.
Contains environmental statements and traffic modelling.
The sheer volume of data creates storage costs and version control issues between the delivery partnership and NH central assurance.
  • Risk Appetite Statement: Defined by the Board to guide operational decisions. Paradoxically, the Board claims a 'low' risk appetite for safety (zero harm), but the legacy infrastructure (concrete roads, unlit sections) requires a 'moderate' operational risk acceptance to keep the network open.
The definitive commercial document closing out a scheme (or RIS period).
For legacy schemes, this is often delayed by years due to unresolved Compensation Events and disputes over 'Pain/Gain' share calculations.
  • Strategic Road Network Initial Report (SRNIR): The statutory document National Highways submits to the DfT to kickstart the RIS setting process. It's the primary output of the Route Strategies. It contains the 'unconstrained view' of network needs, heavily redacted to align with political sensitivities regarding Smart Motorways (ALR) and environmental headwinds. Critically, it outlines the funding gap for Renewals (Operations) vs Enhancements.
The formal output of the Route Strategy process.
It constitutes the 'advice' to the Secretary of State for Transport.
While comprehensive, it represents a 'wish list' before the hard constraints of the RIS Settlement are applied.
  • Optimism Bias Adjustment: A mandatory financial calculation applied to scheme estimates during assurance. Often a point of contention; CPMO enforces higher bias percentages (increasing required budget) which scheme Project Managers fight against to keep projects viable within the RIS envelope.
  • Route Strategy Evidence Reports (18 Regional Bundles): The 18 specific documents (e.g., 'London Orbital', 'South Coast') detailing the state of the network. These are often inconsistent in quality depending on the regional OD's engagement level. They contain the specific tension points: e.g., M60 J8-18 congestion vs. lack of land for widening. These feed the national SRNIR.
  • Incident Timeline Data (LARS): The Logging and Reporting System record used for post-incident debriefs. Data Quality Issue: Start times are often fuzzy (detection vs verification), making 'Response Time' analysis for ORR reporting difficult. LARS relies on operator manual entry, which is often deprioritized during major events.
  • Live Lane Breakdown Classification: High-risk incident type on ALR (All Lane Running) schemes. Frequency is increasing alongside vehicle fleet age. Requires immediate Red X compliance. If detection > 60s, risk of secondary collision spikes exponentially (GG 104 safety risk).
  • Electric Vehicle (EV) Fire Classification: Emerging incident type requiring specific operational protocol (quarantine areas, increased exclusion zones). Current recovery contracts often lack sufficient capability for thermal runaway management, causing prolonged lane closures.
  • Statutory Collision Data (STATS19): Formal record of PI (Personal Injury) collisions. There is a data latency gap between Police recording (CRASH system) and National Highways operational data (HALOGEN), obscuring real-time safety hotspot analysis.
  • JESIP Joint Decision Log: The formal record of decisions made between Police (Gold/Silver command) and NH Traffic Officers (Bronze). Inaccuracies here often lead to post-incident liability disputes regarding who authorized the 'All Stop' versus rolling road block, particularly in Smart Motorway Evidence Stocktake reviews.
  • TMA 2004 Delegated Powers: Specific statutory powers granted to ORTOs: Power to stop and direct traffic (Sections 6/7), power to place temporary traffic signs, and authority to close lanes. Crucial distinction: TOs do NOT have Pace powers (speed enforcement) or power of arrest, creating frequent operational friction with Police forces during 'gold command' incident handovers.
  • Development Consent Order (DCO): The statutory instrument required for Nationally Significant Infrastructure Projects (NSIPs). The 'gold plating' of DCO applications to avoid judicial review is doubling the pre-construction phase duration.

The legal 'permission to build'.

The sheer volume of environmental data and consultation responses required has made this the most complex DCO in UK history, draining resource from other strategic planning activities and highlighting the fragility of the NSIP (Nationally Significant Infrastructure Projects) process.
The statutory planning instrument.
The primary source of schedule slip in the capital programme.
Requires immense data production (Environmental Statements) that are rarely reused for operational baselining.
The statutory instrument granting planning permission for Nationally Significant Infrastructure Projects, essential for schemes like the A428 and A66.
  • Condition Data Set (TRACS/SCRIM): Raw condition data including skid resistance (SCRIM) and ride quality. This data is often siloed before ingestion into HASS, creating a 'swivel chair' integration problem where engineers manually validate anomalies against video footage.
  • National Skid Resistance Survey (SCRIM) Data: distinct dataset from SCANNER, measuring wet-road skid resistance. Highly sensitive data due to legal implications in collision investigations. Failure to process this data within 48 hours of survey creates massive corporate liability exposure under the Highways Act 1980.
  • SRN Pavement Condition Data (SCANNER/TRACS): The raw machine-surveyed data covering rutting, ride quality (longitudinal profile), and texture depth. Collected annually via high-speed survey vehicles. Operational Constraint: There is a 3-6 month lag between data collection and availability in HAPMS/IAM-IS, meaning investment decisions for Year 1 of a delivery cycle are often based on data that is 18 months old, risking scope creep when contractors mobilize.
  • Performance Specification (P50): The primary metric for average delay. Creates a perverse incentive for Regional Directors to delay booking maintenance access (road space booking) to keep lanes open, resulting in a backlog of Category 2 defects and deteriorating Asset Condition (LARS) scores.
  • As-Built Asset Attribute Data: The specific data payload required at handover. Includes safety-critical attributes: Barrier Performance Class (H1/H2), Pavement Material Type (Thin Surface Course vs. Hot Rolled Asphalt), and ITS device IP configurations. Missing attributes here directly impact LARS (Long-term Asset Renewal Strategy) modeling.
  • Bridge Condition Index (BCI) Score: The aggregated metric (0-100) derived from element-level severity and extent scores (CS 450). Critical distinction: BCI_avg (network health) vs. BCI_crit (safety critical elements). A high BCI_avg can hide a failing pier crosshead. Used to justify capital renewal funding in the Road Investment Strategy (RIS).
  • RIS2 Capital Renewals Budget: The ring-fenced funding pot for 2020-2025. Inflation has eroded the real purchasing power, forcing the Deferral of lower-priority schemes (e.g., A14 drainage) to fund overspending on critical structures like the M5 Oldbury Viaduct.
  • Stopped Vehicle Detection (SVD) Data: Real-time alerts generated by radar units on ALR sections. Feeds into NTOC (National Traffic Operations Centre) but suffers from high false alarm rates, causing operator fatigue and reducing trust in the system.
Critical operational data stream derived from radar units on Smart Motorways (ALR).
The signal-to-noise ratio (false alarms) is the primary operational headache, affecting Traffic Officer trust and Control Room workload.
Accuracy is a key ORR compliance metric.
  • Environmental Statement (ES): Legal document detailing impacts. strictly governed by DMRB LA 101-120 standards. Inconsistencies here (e.g., between traffic modeling in the Transport Assessment and noise modeling in the ES) are primary attack vectors during Examination.
The legal document detailing the assessment of environmental impacts for an NSIP.
Includes chapters on Noise, Air Quality, Biodiversity, and Landscape.
Reviewed by the Planning Inspectorate.
  • Statement of Community Consultation (SoCC): A legally binding document defining how NH will consult with the local community. Must be developed in collaboration with Host Local Authorities. If the SoCC is deemed inadequate by Local Authorities, the entire DCO application is at risk of rejection by the Planning Inspectorate (PINS) at the acceptance stage.
  • Consultation Report: The massive evidentiary document (often 1000+ pages) submitted to PINS demonstrating how NH regarded responses under s49 of the Planning Act 2008. This is the primary audit trail defending against accusations of 'predetermination'. It links specific feedback to design changes or justifications for rejection.
  • Preliminary Environmental Information Report (PEIR): Snapshot of the Environmental Statement shared during consultation. A major source of tension: stakeholders treat it as a final design and attack it, while NH engineers view it as a 'worst-case scenario' envelope. Misalignment here drives negative sentiment scores.
  • Traffic Flow Data (MIDAS/TMU): Raw loop data and radar data from MIDAS and Traffic Monitoring Units. Used to calculate 'Delay' and 'Reliability' metrics. Critical tension: Aging inductive loops on the SRN are failing at a rate faster than renewals, leading to data gaps in the Route Strategy evidence base, forcing reliance on third-party floating vehicle data.
  • Carbon Tool v2.5: The mandatory reporting mechanism for supply chain partners. Technically legacy; it's a glorified spreadsheet wrapper that fails to integrate via API with modern BIM (Building Information Modelling) or digital twin platforms. This lack of integration forces manual double-handling of data by contractors, leading to 'reporting fatigue' and data lags of up to a quarter.
Mandatory reporting tool for supply chain to log embodied carbon.
Links to ADMM (Asset Data Management Manual).
Often criticized for data lag; contractors 'game' the inputs to meet KPIs rather than genuinely reducing carbon.
  • Asset Data (ADMM Format): Asset Data Management Manual formatted data. The currency of handover. Innovation trials often produce rich, unstructured data (LiDAR point clouds, video analytics) which gets stripped down to basic ADMM attributes to fit into the legacy HAPMS/IAM IS databases, destroying value.
  • Vehicle Special Order (VSO): The bureaucratic instrument used by the DfT/VCA to legally permit the trials. It serves as a temporary suspension of specific construction and use regulations. This creates a fragmented enforcement environment for NH Traffic Officers, who must distinguish between a 'legal' trial VSO scooter and an 'illegal' private scooter on the Strategic Road Network, often with no visual distinction at 60mph.
  • Departure from Standards (DAS): The formal record authorizing non-compliance with DMRB (e.g., CD 377 for barriers or CD 529 for drainage). Used by Major Projects to legitimize value engineering decisions, effectively codifying the transfer of risk to Operations.
  • Strategic Supply Chain Risk Register: The 'dirty laundry' list of single-source dependencies. Includes specific risks like the monopoly on Dynac/CHARM traffic management software (Kapsch) and the scarcity of specialized tunneling labor for the Lower Thames Crossing. It is rarely fully transparent to the DfT due to political sensitivity.
  • Project Bank Accounts (PBA) Mechanism: Financial instrument mandated to protect Tier 2/3 subcontractors from Tier 1 insolvency. While policy dictates their use, Tier 1 contractors frequently resist or delay implementation to maintain their own working capital flows, creating hidden friction in the supply chain.
The financial mechanism ensuring Tier 2/3 suppliers get paid.
Commercial monitors this strictly to prevent Tier 1s from holding cash, but setting these up for complex JVs (like on A66) is an administrative burden that slows down mobilization.
  • Application for Payment (AfP): The monthly data submission from the Tier 1 contractor detailing costs incurred. Must be substantiated by timesheets, invoices, and Goods Received Notes. Inaccurate AfPs are the primary trigger for commercial reject notices.
  • Hazard Log (GDMS/SharePoint): The live repository of identified risks for specific schemes. Often fragmented across disparate systems (legacy spreadsheets vs. new GDMS instances), leading to poor visibility of systemic risks across the SRN.
  • STATS19 Collision Data: Standardised police collision reporting data used as the primary input for cluster identification. A critical operational constraint is the lag time (often 6-12 months) between incident occurrence and validated data availability in NH systems, forcing architects to rely on raw control centre logs (HALOGEN) for immediate post-incident analysis despite lower data quality.
Standardised accident reporting dataset provided by the Police.
The lag in validation (often 6-12 months) creates a 'knowledge blind spot' for immediate operational trend analysis.
Standardized dataset of personal injury road accidents, supplied by police forces (via CRASH/COPA) and critical for calculating Killed or Seriously Injured (KSI) metrics.
  • Health and Safety File (H&S File): The repository of residual risk data (e.g., hazardous materials, maintenance access limitations) required for future operations. This is the 'hot potato' of the PCF Stage 6 Handover; if not digitized correctly for ingestion into HART/IAM IS, it becomes 'dead data' in a PDF, creating latent safety risks for Traffic Officers and maintenance crews.
  • Protected Species License (Great Crested Newt/Badger): Legal permits from Natural England. These have hard seasonal constraints (e.g., you can't move newts in winter). Missing a window by one week can delay a RIS scheme (like A417 Missing Link) by a full year, wrecking the P50 delivery confidence.
  • Carbon Tool (Carbon Calculation Platform): The mandated calculation engine for estimating scheme carbon. It relies on standard emissions factors that often lag behind material innovation, creating a perverse incentive where contractors use better materials but can't claim the 'carbon credit' in the official score.
  • International Benchmarking Report 2024: A politically sensitive document used to justify RIS3 funding requests to the Office of Rail and Road (ORR). It selectively compares NH performance (P50 Network Availability) against international peers to demonstrate efficiency, often glossing over the structural differences in network age and legacy debt.
  • Development Consent Order (DCO) Requirements: The legal constraints for NSIPs (A66, A428, LTC). These contain 'hard' commitments to stakeholders that the supply chain must deliver. Often, the supply chain prices the works before fully understanding the DCO environmental constraints, leading to cost shocks later.
Legal planning instruments for NSIPs (Nationally Significant Infrastructure Projects).
We are currently retrofitting digital connectivity requirements into the DCOs for the Lower Thames Crossing and A66 to ensure we don't build 'dumb' concrete that needs digging up in 5 years for fibre.
  • Social Value Delivery Plan (Contractual): The binding document within the NEC4 contract. It details specific commitments to TOMs measures. A critical friction point: contractors often 'gold plate' this at bid stage (to win the 10% score) but struggle to deliver practically once site mobilization begins.
  • Pavement Condition Data (SCANNER/LARS): The raw physics of the road. Laser surface operational data measuring rutting, texture, and cracking. Crucial for determining the 'Road Surface Condition' (RSC) KPI. Discrepancies between this data and actual driver experience (potholes) create significant reputational risk.
  • Stopped Vehicle Detection (SVD) Radar Telemetry: High-velocity data stream from radar units on ALR sections (e.g., M6 J13-15). The signal-to-noise ratio is a major architectural problem; false positives from rain or vegetation trigger alarms in RCCs, desensitizing operators and risking missed genuine incidents.
  • Development Consent Order (DCO) Application Data: Massive datasets (thousands of PDFs, geospatial maps, environmental statements) for NSIPs like Lower Thames Crossing. The digital presentation of this object determines the validity of the statutory consultation process under the Planning Act 2008.
Massive legal documentation sets for NSIPs like the A66.
Stored in ProjectWise; strictly version-controlled to avoid judicial review risks.
The massive legal instrument (often 50,000+ pages) comprising the Draft DCO, Environmental Statement, Book of Reference, and Consultation Report.
Must comply with APFP Regulations.
The legal instrument for NSIPs.
Becoming increasingly difficult to secure due to environmental challenges, causing planning blight and freezing capital that should be deployed.
Recent legal challenges (climate change grounds on A303 Stonehenge) have forced Architecture to heavily document carbon baselines in PAS 2080, adding months to the pre-construction phase.
