Suppliers often struggle to align their proprietary risk tools with GG 104's specific safety risk characterisation, leading to assurance delays at the Technical Assurance Board (TAB).
Governs the safety case for any changes introduced by the supply chain.
This is the 'hard gate' that often halts innovation trials from the supply chain because the bureaucratic burden of proof is too high for SMEs.
The 'Generic Governance' standard that dictates As Low As Reasonably Practicable (ALARP) logic.
The weaponisation of this standard by internal safety governance often freezes operational changes due to the difficulty in quantifying safety deltas for new digital interventions.
The overarching safety standard requiring 'As Low As Reasonably Practicable' (ALARP) demonstration.
Heavily influences the design approval of the A303 Stonehenge and A428 schemes.
DMRB standard providing the framework for identifying hazards and assessing risks in civil engineering and maintenance activities.
DMRB standard defining the 'As Low As Reasonably Practicable' (ALARP) framework and safety risk governance for all changes to the SRN, critical for Smart Motorway safety cases.
  • TSRGD (Traffic Signs Regulations and General Directions) 2016: The rigid legal framework that prevents NH from simply adopting international innovation. For example, while Rijkswaterstaat uses specific red-X signalling, NH cannot deploy identical solutions without DfT legislative changes, creating a 'digital lag' between international best practice and UK implementation.
Legal statutory instrument dictating what can be shown on a VMS.
Strictly limits the 'free text' messages RCCs can set, often preventing specific, helpful driver information during unique incidents.
Statutory legal constraint.
Dictates exactly what can be displayed on a VMS (Variable Message Sign).
It severely limits the ability of the Digital Roads team to innovate with new driver information formats without seeking DfT authorization.
Legal constraint.
Strictly limits what messages can be displayed on VMS.
Prevents NTIS from displaying 'informal' routing advice or specific commercial brand names during major incidents.
This regulatory rigidity clashes with the flexible messaging capabilities of modern VMS hardware.
Statutory instrument.
It dictates exactly what we can display on Variable Message Signs (VMS).
It limits our ability to innovate with new driver information messages without DfT authorization.
Statutory instrument defining the legal status of the Red X.
If our architectural systems (NTIS/HART) cannot log the exact time a sign was lit, police cannot prosecute non-compliance, undermining the safety case for ALR.
  • Construction Design and Management (CDM) Regulations 2015: Statutory instrument mandating health and safety management in construction. For National Highways, this is not just compliance; it dictates the legal liability structure for NSIPs (e.g., Lower Thames Crossing) and regional renewals. Failure here triggers HSE intervention and ORR regulatory escalation.
  • Supply Chain Health Data GDPR Barriers: Legal barrier preventing National Highways from directly accessing granular health data of Tier 2/3 contractors working on schemes like the M6 J21a-26 Smart Motorway upgrade.
  • Development Consent Order (DCO): Legal planning instrument. Once sealed for schemes like LTC, it freezes scope. Any innovation or operational change post-DCO requires heavy legal lifting, discouraging agility.
  • Public Contracts Regulations (PCR) 2015: Statutory instrument dictating procurement procedures. Limits the ability to directly award work to performing contractors without re-tendering, often delaying remedial works.
The statutory framework governing NH procurement.
Strict adherence often slows down the 'agility' requested by Digital Roads initiatives, creating a trade-off between speed of innovation (e.g., trialling new roadside technology) and legal compliance risk.
  • Environment Act 2021 (BNG Mandate): Statutory requirement forcing minimum 10% Biodiversity Net Gain on all NSIPs (e.g., Lower Thames Crossing, A66) and Town & Country Planning Act consents. Creates a 'hard constraint' on Red Line Boundaries—if the Defra Metric 4.0 calculation is negative, the DCO application is at significant risk of rejection by the Planning Inspectorate.
  • ORR Safety & Performance Monitor Mandate: Statutory oversight from the Office of Rail and Road. They actively monitor the 'Smart Motorways Stocktake' delivery. If NH fails to meet availability metrics or SVD performance targets, the ORR can intervene, altering capital allocation priorities away from enhancements toward renewals.
  • The Road Tunnel Safety Regulations 2007 (Retained EU Law): The absolute statutory baseline for SRN tunnel operations, deriving from the EU Directive 2004/54/EC. It designates the Administrative Authority (DfT) and mandates the appointments of Tunnel Managers and Safety Officers. Non-compliance here isn't a KPI failure; it's a legal breach. This constrains every decision made at RCC Godstone regarding the A3 Hindhead or the A282 Dartford Crossing.
  • Vienna Convention on Road Traffic (1968): International treaty requirement that a driver must be in control of the vehicle at all times. The primary legal barrier to Level 3+ autonomy. UK derogation is required to allow 'hands-off' operation, creating a friction point between vehicle capability and legal road use.
  • Road Tunnel Safety Regulations 2007: Statutory instrument implementing the EU Directive on tunnel safety. Non-compliance carries criminal liability for the Tunnel Manager, overriding any corporate efficiency targets.
  • DMRB CD 352 Design of Road Tunnels: The governing standard for tunnel safety systems. Strict requirements for ventilation and fire suppression. Operational friction: Retrofitting CD 352 compliance onto aging assets like the A282 Dartford tunnels is technically nearly impossible without total reconstruction, forcing reliance on 'ALARP' (As Low As Reasonably Practicable) risk arguments via GG 104 assessments.
Current design standard.
Impossible to retrofit to assets like Blackwall or Dartford West without total reconstruction.
Creates a permanent 'Departures from Standard' administrative burden for asset engineers.
Standard mandating safety systems and ventilation requirements.
Rigid adherence often conflicts with the practicalities of retrofitting legacy assets like the Dartford Crossing, requiring complex Departures from Standards.
The DMRB standard governing tunnel safety.
Currently creating friction for the A303 Stonehenge scheme where heritage constraints conflict with standard emergency exit spacing requirements.
Strict prescriptive standard defining tunnel safety systems.
Clashes with legacy infrastructure in older tunnels (like Dartford), creating 'departures from standard' paperwork nightmares.
  • UNECE Regulation 157 (ALKS): Automated Lane Keeping System regulation. This creates a massive operational headache for our Traffic Officers and NTOC, as it legally defines when a 'driver' is not driving. We currently lack the real-time telemetry to know if a vehicle involved in an incident on the M1 or M25 was in ALKS mode, complicating liability and clearance under the Traffic Management Act 2004.
Automated Lane Keeping System technical regulation.
Limits initial automated driving to 37mph (later 80mph) on motorways without pedestrians.
NH must certify specific SRN sections as 'ODD-compliant' (Operational Design Domain) for this regulation to apply.
  • DMRB GG 104: Requirements for safety risk assessment. The holy grail of NH engineering governance. The tension here is extreme: trying to apply GG 104's deterministic 'As Low As Reasonably Practicable' (ALARP) logic to the probabilistic, non-deterministic nature of AI-driven CAVs defined in ISO 21448. This mismatch stalls safety cases for trials.
The absolute gatekeeper for any CAV trial on the SRN.
The rigorous ALARP (As Low As Reasonably Practicable) requirements for unproven autonomous software create significant project delays, often conflicting with agile development cycles of CAV partners.
  • NCSC Cyber Assessment Framework (CAF) Profile B: Mandatory security standard for Critical National Infrastructure. The friction point is 'Data Process Security'—legacy roadside technology was never designed to meet modern encryption standards, forcing expensive retrofits or risk acceptance waivers that the DfT is increasingly reluctant to sign off.
Mandatory compliance framework for Critical National Infrastructure (CNI).
National Highways must demonstrate 'Manage', 'Protect', 'Detect', and 'Minimize' capabilities.
Creates significant tension with legacy Operational Technology (OT) assets (e.g., specific tunnel SCADA systems dating back to 2005) which cannot meet modern encryption or logging standards without total replacement.
  • Civil Service Pay Band Structure: HR constraint preventing National Highways from offering market-rate salaries for Data Scientists and V2X Cyber Security specialists, who command 40-60% higher wages in the private sector. This forces a heavy reliance on the TSSP (Technical Specialist and Surveyor Framework) supply chain, hollowing out institutional memory.
Government-mandated salary bands and FTE caps that create severe friction in recruiting Data Architects and Cyber Security specialists, forcing National Highways to rely on expensive contingent labour (contractors) for critical systems like HART and NTIS.
  • Managing Public Money (MPM): HMT's statutory framework. It strictly prohibits cross-subsidisation between financial years ('annuality') and between Capital and Resource budgets. This forces a 'use it or lose it' mentality in Q4, often leading to inefficient, rushed spending on minor works just to balance the books.
  • Net Zero Carbon 2030 (Corporate Estate): Mandatory decarbonization target for NH's own estate (Scope 1 & 2). Forces capital trade-offs: funding diverted from operational capacity upgrades to retrofitting heat pumps and LED lighting at depots like Bescot and motorway service areas.
  • NIS Directive & CNI Security Segmentation: Statutory obligation to protect Critical National Infrastructure. This creates a friction point for the Digital Workplace programme, effectively preventing a 'single pane of glass' view for Traffic Officers who have to use separate terminals for corporate email (O365) and command and control (HART). We are trading user experience for national security.
  • Section 43 (Commercial Interests) Exemption: The primary shield used to redact procurement data for the Lower Thames Crossing and A303 Stonehenge. While necessary to protect negotiation positions with Tier 1 contractors, it creates significant friction with transparency groups and leads to frequent ICO appeals.
  • HMT Green Book (Five Case Model): Mandates Strategic, Economic, Commercial, Financial, and Management cases. The 'Optimism Bias' adjustment required here is the primary reason the A66 budget looks insufficient before ground is even broken.
  • RIS2 Funding Envelope: The fixed 5-year budget settlement from DfT; currently oversubscribed due to inflation, forcing the IPC to make trade-offs between enhancement schemes (A66) and maintenance (Renewals).
  • ORR Safety & Performance Targets: Regulatory constraints from the Office of Rail and Road. Includes targets for KSI (Killed or Seriously Injured) reduction and Network Availability. Failure to meet these triggers heavy scrutiny and potential fines. This drives a risk-averse culture where innovation (like removing hard shoulders) is politically toxic.
  • ORR Regulatory Investigation 2024: Office of Rail and Road intervention regarding the failure to deliver SVD performance targets. This external pressure forces NH to prioritize tech fixes over other operational improvements.
  • DNO Grid Capacity Constraints: The primary blocker for EV infrastructure. Remote MSAs (e.g., Tebay, Killington Lake) require multi-million pound transmission reinforcements from DNOs (ENWL, NGED) that exceed the commercial viability of the charging revenue alone.
  • Legacy Funding Model (Capex vs Opex): The Treasury rule that allows borrowing for 'New' digital schemes (RIS Capital) but restricts funding for maintaining the systems they create (Resource/Opex). This structural flaw creates 'zombie projects'—pilot mobility services that launch successfully but die because Operations cannot afford the cloud hosting or software licensing fees after handover.
  • RIS2 Capital Baseline: The rigid financial envelope (2020-2025) defined by DfT. Inflationary pressure on schemes like A66 Northern Trans-Pennine is forcing a trade-off: either de-scope the technology (reducing future operational capability) or delay the opening date (missing RIS commitments).
  • Road Investment Strategy 2 (RIS2) Budget Envelope: The rigid funding settlement (2020-2025). Inflation (construction materials) has rendered the original scope undeliverable. This forces the 'Tier 0' decision board to descale projects or push them to RIS3, creating 'zombie schemes' that exist on paper but have no delivery route.
  • DMRB Update Latency: The time lag between identifying a systemic issue (via DfS or Incident) and publishing an updated standard (e.g., GG 104). Can take 18-24 months, leaving a window of operational risk.
  • NH Board Risk Appetite Statement: Formal quantitative and qualitative boundaries for risk acceptance. Creates significant tension in the Major Projects Directorate where 'Zero Harm' safety appetite conflicts with the 'Construction Cost' appetite required to deliver schemes like the A66 Northern Trans-Pennine within the RIS envelope.
  • Legacy Operational Technology Debt: The burden of maintaining aging roadside technology (MIDAS radar, emergency roadside telephones) which consumes OPEX capability. This constraint actively inhibits the 'Response Development' to digital megatrends because the budget is consumed by maintaining end-of-life assets to satisfy ORR availability targets.
  • ORR Efficiency KPI Target: The regulatory requirement to deliver >£2bn efficiency savings in RIS2/3. This drives the architectural push for 'standardised designs' and 'digital twins', but often results in 'value engineering' that strips out future-proofing resilience (e.g., reducing drainage capacity to minimum CD 529 standards).
  • TSRGD 2016 Compliance: Statutory legal instrument dictating signal aspects. HART contains hard-coded logic to prevent illegal signal combinations (e.g., specific speeds alongside Red X). This logic makes HART rigid; implementing new signal types for Digital Roads initiatives requires deep-code intervention, slowing down innovation cycles.
  • DMRB GG 128 Requirements for reporting and recording incidents: The governing standard that dictates the data schema and process workflows configured within HART. It mandates the categorization of events (e.g., AIRS categories), the 20-day investigation timeline for severe incidents, and the specific requirements for reporting 'incursions' into traffic management. Non-compliance here creates direct legal exposure under the Health and Safety at Work Act 1974.
  • PAS 2080 Carbon Management Standard: Requires supply chain to evidence whole-life carbon reduction. Creates a massive trade-off tension: low-carbon concrete alternatives often require longer curing times, conflicting with 'Open for Traffic' P50 schedule commitments.
  • 5.9 GHz ITS Spectrum Reservation: Spectrum band (5.875-5.905 GHz) reserved for Intelligent Transport Systems (ITS-G5 / C-V2X). A strategic architectural battleground: NH requires this for future Connected and Autonomous Vehicle (CAV) safety messaging (DMRB GG 104 safety cases), while the tech industry lobbies Ofcom to open this band for generic Wi-Fi expansion, threatening potential interference with safety-critical V2X pilots.
  • Ofcom Spectrum Licensing: Regulatory constraints managing interference and allocation. NH must pay for licenses for microwave links and adhere to power limits on the 5.8 GHz band to avoid interference with satellite services.
  • RIS2 Capital Enhancement Budget (Roads Period 2): The fixed funding envelope allocated by DfT for the 2020-2025 period, acting as a hard financial constraint on pavement renewal volumes and the Legacy Concrete Roads reconstruction programme.
  • Network Occupancy Management (NROC): Operational constraints managed by the Network Occupancy Management System (NOMS/NROC), limiting when pavement works can occur (e.g., Chapter 8 lane closures) to minimize delay minutes and journey time reliability impact.
  • DMRB GG 103 Sustainable Development and Design: Standards mandate requiring design life assessment, focusing on material efficiency, durability, and adaptability for future generations.
The governing standard mandating the inclusion of sustainable development in design decision-making.
Often conflicts with GG 104 (Safety Risk) when proposing reduced lighting or narrower cross-sections to save embodied carbon.

ARCHIMATE:CONTRACT (10 components):
---------------------------------
  • Area 10/12 Asset Delivery Maintenance Contracts: Term maintenance contracts for the North West and Yorkshire. These contracts are critical for maintaining LARS (Lane Availability Reporting System) data and delivering winter service duties. Friction exists here: while NH defines *what* needs doing (Maintenance Requirements), the contractor holds the plant and labour, creating bottlenecks during severe weather events.
  • Regional Delivery Partnership (RDP) Framework: The primary vehicle for delivering RIS2 schemes >£100m. Unlike previous transactional contracts, RDP attempts to incentivise outcomes, but struggles with the practical application of the Collaborative Performance Framework (CPF) when supply chain margins are squeezed. Governs delivery of schemes like the M6 Junction 19-21a upgrades.
The primary contractual vehicle for RIS2 major schemes.
It uses NEC4 ECC Option C (Target Cost with Activity Schedule).
It was designed to incentivize collaboration, but in reality, often devolves into arguments over Disallowed Cost and Pain/Gain share when scope creep occurs due to evolving DMRB standards (e.g., GG 104 safety risk requirements).
  • NRTS2 Telecommunications Service Agreement: The backbone contract (currently Telent) providing connectivity for 30,000+ roadside assets to the RCCs and NTOC. A major constraint is the rigidity of this PFI-style arrangement, which makes agile deployment of new sensor types (e.g., for Stopped Vehicle Detection validation) slow and legally complex.
  • Collaborative Delivery Framework (CDF): The heavy hitters. Tier 1 framework (Balfour Beatty, Costain, Skanska, etc.) for schemes >£100m. Designed for Early Contractor Involvement (ECI), but often treated as a transactional pricing mechanism by procurement, undermining the 'Collaborative' title.
  • NEC4 Engineering and Construction Contract (A428): The commercial battleground. Target Cost contract with Activity Schedule. Tension exists over 'Pain/Gain' share mechanisms as inflation pushes actual costs above the target, incentivizing the contractor to flood the Project Manager with Early Warning Notices (EWNs).
  • NEC4 Option C (Target Contract with Activity Schedule): The standard commercial vehicle for major schemes like A66 and A428. Theoretically shares pain/gain, but in reality, compensation events regarding ground conditions often shift the risk back to National Highways, eroding the 'efficiency' savings claimed in the RIS delivery plan.
The specific contractual mechanism governing the pain/gain share.
Includes Clauses for Compensation Events (CEs) and Early Warnings (EWNs).
This is where the friction lives: Commercial pushes for strict adherence to the defined cost, while Major Projects argues for scope changes to meet 'Open for Traffic' dates.
  • M25 DBFO Contract (Connect Plus): Legacy 30-year Design-Build-Finance-Operate PFI contract ending 2039. This is an architectural straightjacket; while it transfers risk, it locks National Highways into rigid payment mechanisms indexed to availability. It complicates the rollout of new digital standards (e.g., migrating M25 signals to HART) because contract variations are prohibitively expensive.
  • Road Investment Strategy 2 (RIS2): The £27.4bn statutory funding instrument (2020-2025). It legally commits us to enhancements like A303 Stonehenge and A428, creating a rigid capital envelope that leaves little flexibility when inflation spikes construction costs.
The £27.4bn statutory investment plan (2020-2025) mandating delivery of schemes like A303 Stonehenge and Lower Thames Crossing.
Funding is ring-fenced for Capital (Capex), creating a tension where new assets are built but Resource (Opex) for maintenance is constrained.
  • Regional Delivery Partnership (RDP): The primary delivery vehicle for RIS2 Regional Investment Programme (RIP) schemes (e.g., A428 Black Cat). Utilizes 'Delivery Integration Partners' (DIPs) like Skanska or Costain. Structurally creates a tension where DIPs are incentivised on 'outcomes' but constrained by legacy DMRB standards.
  • Scheme Delivery Framework (SDF): Replaced the Asset Support Delivery Framework (ASDF). Used primarily for Operations renewals (pavement, barriers, drainage). Divides England into super-regions, but creates interface friction with RDP schemes where geographic boundaries overlap (e.g., M6 operational maintenance vs. major upgrade works).

ARCHIMATE:COURSEOFACTION (17 components):
---------------------------------------
  • RIS3 (Road Investment Strategy 3) Pipeline Planning: The strategic investment plan for 2025-2030. Currently under immense pressure to pivot from 'new capacity' to 'renewals and maintenance' due to the crumbling concrete roads estate (e.g., A12, A14) and inflation eroding buying power.
  • CAV Infrastructure Readiness Strategy (RIS3): Strategic alignment of digital initiatives within Road Investment Strategy 3 (RIS3) to support SAE Level 3/4 automation. Focuses on the transition from physical-heavy infrastructure to digital-twin enabled operations, specifically targeting the A2/M2 and A66 Northern Trans-Pennine schemes. Addresses the tension between sinking capital into roadside hardware (RSUs) versus relying on cellular (C-V2X) coverage.
  • DfT Future of Transport Regulatory Review (Micromobility Sandbox): The specific regulatory sandbox mechanism (2020-2024) utilizing Vehicle Special Orders (VSOs) under the Road Traffic Act 1988 to bypass primary legislation restrictions. Operationally, this delegates authority to Local Transport Authorities (e.g., West Midlands, Tees Valley) to trial e-scooters and delivery bots. For National Highways, this creates a critical operational friction at the SRN interface (e.g., A-road crossings, grade-separated junctions like M6 J6), requiring us to assess how non-standard vehicle dynamics interact with standard DMRB CD 123 geometric designs.
  • Road Investment Strategy 2 (RIS2) Delivery Plan: The current 5-year strategic plan (£27.4bn). Heavily skewed towards capital enhancements (A303 Stonehenge, A428 Black Cat) at the expense of the renewal backlog, creating friction in asset lifecycle management.
  • Project Speed Efficiency Initiative (CIP Acceleration): Government-mandated initiative to cut NSIP delivery timelines by 33-50%. For National Highways, this specifically targeted the DCO pre-application phase for the Complex Infrastructure Programme (CIP). It forced a move from sequential to parallel design/consultation workflows but created significant friction with Statutory Environmental Bodies (SEBs) who lacked resource to match the tempo.
  • Digital Roads Strategy 2025: The 'Vision 2050' shift from concrete to data. Aims to implement Connected and Autonomous Plant (CAP) and Digital Twins, but is severely constrained by the technical debt in the HART and CHARM legacy stacks.
  • 60mph Speed Limit Enforcement (Air Quality): Operational intervention on M1, M6, and M602 to reduce NO2 emissions. This is a 'soft' infrastructure solution to a 'hard' chemical problem. It artificially suppresses the 'Average Speed' metric, negatively impacting the Road Period performance reporting to the ORR.
  • Digital Roads Strategy Implementation: Strategic shift from physical to digital infrastructure. Aims to implement V2X and digital twins. Often conflicts with the 'potholes first' political pressure and maintenance backlog funding.
The strategic response to technological acceleration.
Includes initiatives like the A2/M2 Connected Corridor.
However, implementation is often fragmented across regions, leading to interoperability issues between the National Traffic Operations Centre (NTOC) and regional control systems.
  • Digital Roads Upskilling Programme: Strategic initiative to pivot the workforce from 'operating the road' to 'managing the system'. Includes retrofitting data literacy into the Traffic Officer curriculum and establishing the Digital Academy. Critical for the transition from NTOC legacy systems to the new HART (Highways Agency Road Management System) architecture.
  • HART (Highways Agency Roadside Technology) Transformation Programme: The critical migration from legacy NRTS (National Roads Telecommunications Services) transmission contracts to a sovereign, IP-based fibre operational technology network. This is not just IT; it's the nervous system for roadside assets. Delays here actively block the 'Digital Roads' vision by trapping us in TDM (Time Division Multiplexing) legacy constraints. Essential for supporting future V2X and reducing dependency on proprietary telco operational envelopes.
  • Digital Roads Strategy (Connected Corridors): The strategic ambition for 2025-2030, focusing on V2X (Vehicle-to-Infrastructure) readiness and GLOSA (Green Light Optimization Speed Advisory). Currently a 'paper tiger' until HART infrastructure is live on key corridors like the M25 and M6. It competes for R&D funding with immediate safety retrofits required by the Smart Motorway stocktake.
  • Asset Management Transformation (IAM IS & dDSS): Moving from cyclical renewal to predictive maintenance using the Integrated Asset Management Information System (IAM IS). The trade-off is the immense data cleansing burden required to make legacy 'as-built' drawings (often PDF or paper) compatible with BIM Level 3 standards and the decision support tools (dDSS) defined in ADMM.
  • Net Zero Highways Plan (Carbon Management): Strategic drive to decarbonise the SRN by 2050. Immediate operational impact is the 'Project Rapid' rollout of EV charging infrastructure at motorway service areas, which is currently constrained by DNO (Distribution Network Operator) grid capacity and the physical space constraints at legacy service sites.
  • Operation Brock / Stack: Contingency for cross-channel disruption (M20). A prime example of BCP becoming 'Business as Usual'. Requires massive resource diversion from other regions, degrading national resilience.
  • Operation Brock (M20): Contraflow system for cross-channel disruption. A massive operational trade-off: sacrifices local network availability (Kent resilience) to store international freight. Consumes massive Traffic Officer resources, stripping coverage from other parts of the Area 4 network.
  • Project Speed (DCO Acceleration): DfT-mandated initiative to slash delivery times for NSIPs (Nationally Significant Infrastructure Projects). It attempts to compress the DCO (Development Consent Order) pre-application phase. Tensions arise where this speed conflicts with the rigorous statutory environmental consultation requirements for complex schemes like the A66 Northern Trans-Pennine.
  • Legacy Concrete Roads Reconstruction Programme: A specific £400m allocated programme to repair or replace life-expired concrete road surfaces (often 40+ years old) on the SRN (e.g., A11, A14) to improve noise and safety.

ARCHIMATE:DATAOBJECT (11 components):
-----------------------------------
  • Raw Surface Condition Data (HDF5/XML): Terabytes of raw profilometry and imagery data. Contains specific datasets for Texture Depth (MPD), Rut Depth, and Longitudinal Profile (Variance). Data quality often degrades at junctions and roundabouts, creating 'blind spots' in the automated analysis.
  • Floating Vehicle Data (FVD) Stream: Probe data purchased from aggregators (INRIX, TomTom, Google). Essential for coverage on non-sensorised A-roads (A303, A66). Architectural trade-off: High latency (2-3 mins) makes it unsuitable for real-time safety signalling (AID) under TSRGD, but vital for Journey Time Reliability metrics reported to ORR.
  • DSSAD (Data Storage System for Automated Driving) Telemetry: The 'black box' data required to determine liability. NH currently has no direct access to this. We rely on police seizure, slowing down 'Green Claims' (damage recovery) for barrier repairs, impacting the regional renewals budget.
  • As-Built BIM Data (COBie): The digital representation of the physical road. Often delivered months post-construction with missing attributes, making immediate maintenance planning in IAM IS impossible.
  • Traffic Telemetry Data: Real-time flow and speed data generated by MIDAS loops, radar, and Stopped Vehicle Detection (SVD) systems. Essential for Smart Motorway safety algorithms and setting variable speed limits.
  • Incident Log Record: Structured data detailing SRN incidents, including start time, severity (Fatal/Serious/Slight), response resources (Traffic Officers/Emergency Services), and clearance times. Used for measuring Network Availability and Safety Performance.
  • Road Space Booking: A confirmed reservation of specific SRN lanes or carriageways for works. Includes temporal constraints, traffic management type (e.g., contraflow), and impact assessments.
  • Geotechnical Asset Score: Condition grading for earthworks (e.g., Class 1A to 5). Derived from principal inspections and used to prioritize renewals funding to prevent slope failures.
  • Structure Inspection Certificate: Formal record of visual and testing inspections on bridges and gantries, compliant with CS 450. Contains defect descriptions and severity scores feeding into the BCI.
  • Pavement Condition Index: Aggregated metrics for rutting, texture depth, and ride quality (LARS/SCRIM) derived from machine surveys. Determines skid resistance compliance against CS 228.
  • Datex II Data Feed: Standardized XML traffic data schema used to export NTIS network status to third-party aggregators (Google, Waze, TomTom) and Local Highway Authorities.

ARCHIMATE:DELIVERABLE (1 components):
------------------------------------
  • Strategic Road Network Initial Report (SRNIR): The 'Ask'. Published May 2023. Sets out NH's view of the network needs. It explicitly highlights the tension between 'maintain' vs 'enhance'. It leans heavily on DMRB GG 104 safety risk assessments to justify renewals over new capacity.

ARCHIMATE:DRIVER (20 components):
-------------------------------
  • Smart Motorway Evidence Stocktake (2020/2022): The massive strategic pivot point. Following the TSC report and paused rollout of ALR (All Lane Running), Route Strategies had to be completely rewritten to remove planned Smart Motorway schemes. This left a massive gap in how to address congestion on the M1 and M6, leading to a scramble for 'Digital Roads' alternatives that are not yet proven.
The political fallout from All Lane Running (ALR) safety concerns.
This has paralyzed confidence in deploying new technology without 100% proven safety cases.
It creates a massive friction against deploying experimental CAV infrastructure (e.g., digitized TROs) on the live network.
  • Capital Efficiency (RIS Efficiency KPI): The strict Treasury and DfT requirement to deliver schemes within the committed RIS2/RIS3 funding envelope. Drives aggressive descoping of 'gold-plated' assets, often misidentifying essential resilience features as 'nice-to-haves'.
  • RIS2/RIS3 Innovation Designated Funds: Ring-fenced DfT funding (distinct from the main Block Grant). While it funds pilots like the A2/M2 Connected Corridor, the separation from core Maintenance & Renewal (M&R) budgets creates a 'Valley of Death' where successful trials fail to transition into Business As Usual due to lack of operational expenditure (OPEX) cover.
  • Net Zero 2050 Carbon Mandate: Statutory obligation to decarbonise. Fundamentally conflicts with the RIS2 goal of increasing capacity via road widening (e.g., A66 Northern Trans-Pennine).
  • Freight Capacity Demand: Projected 30% increase in freight traffic. Puts immense pressure on pavement assets (skid resistance) and drives the economic case for widening schemes like the Lower Thames Crossing.
  • Cabinet Office PPN 06/20 (Social Value): Mandatory government procurement policy requiring a minimum 10% weighting for social value in all central government contracts, fundamentally altering how we evaluate Tier 1 bids for schemes like A66 and LTC.
  • RIS2 (Road Investment Strategy 2) Commitments: The statutory investment plan (2020-2025). Operations are currently skewed by the need to 'burn' capital budget on schemes like A428 Black Cat before the period ends, often at the expense of revenue-funded maintenance which is severely squeezed.
  • Civil Engineering Competence Scarcity: Critical shortage of Senior Quantity Surveyors and Project Directors capable of managing complex DCO requirements. Creates a 'war for talent' between National Highways, HS2, and Nuclear sectors, impacting delivery confidence on the Lower Thames Crossing.
  • Biodiversity Net Gain (BNG) Mandate: The requirement (Environment Act 2021) to deliver 10% net gain on schemes like A428 Black Cat and A66 Northern Trans-Pennine. Creates massive land-take operational overhead, forcing the acquisition of land solely for offsetting rather than SRN operations, complicating the asset database in HADDMS.
The Environment Act 2021 requirement for +10% gain.
It is fundamentally incompatible with the land-take requirements of traditional lane widening.
Forces us to buy off-site credits or purchase expensive adjacent land, skewing the BCR (Benefit-Cost Ratio).
  • IT/OT Convergence Friction: The cultural and technical war between Enterprise IT (Office 365, Cloud, Security First) and Operational Technology (Roadside, Legacy protocols, Availability First). IT wants to patch systems weekly; OT Operations refuses downtime. This deadlock prevents rapid rollout of digital twin capabilities.
  • Post-ALR Public Trust Deficit: The catastrophic drop in public confidence following the Smart Motorway (All Lane Running) rollout and subsequent 2023 cancellation of new schemes. This creates a severe drag on RIS3 Digital Roads ambitions. Public equates 'Digital/Automated' with 'unsafe removal of hard shoulders'. The operational friction here is palpable—we cannot deploy V2X pilots without overcoming the perception that technology is a cost-cutting measure rather than a safety enhancement.
  • Climate Acceleration & Extreme Weather: Megatrend impacting SRN resilience. Specifically, the increasing frequency of 1-in-100-year storm events overwhelming drainage standards defined in DMRB CG 501. This drives the urgent need for Geotechnical Asset Management (GAM) reform, particularly for slope stability on aging assets like the M62 and A66 Northern Trans-Pennine route.
  • SRN Climate Resilience Imperative (Extreme Weather): The forcing function requiring adaptation of the Strategic Road Network to 4°C global heating scenarios. specifically addressing sea-level rise affecting coastal assets (A55, A259), scour risk at estuary crossings (Severn, Humber), and heat-induced pavement failure on concrete legacy sections (A14, M25). Drives the requirement for retrofitting drainage capacity beyond historical 1-in-100 year return periods.
  • Urbanisation and Orbital Congestion: Shift from long-distance strategic travel to short-hop orbital commuting (M25, M60, M42). This degrades the 'Strategic' nature of the SRN, turning motorways into local distributors, accelerating pavement wear (rutting) and reducing the validity of P50 journey time reliability metrics which weren't designed for stop-start urban friction.
  • Connected & Autonomous Vehicle (CAV) Disruption: Technological acceleration forcing a shift from physical infrastructure (tarmac/steel) to digital infrastructure. Creates tension between maintaining legacy operational tech (HALOGEN signs, copper loops) and investing in V2X readiness. Heavily influences the 'Digital Roads' vision but is constrained by the reality of the National Traffic Information Service (NTIS) legacy architecture.
  • Ageing Road User Demographic (>70s): The fastest-growing SRN user segment. This creates a tangible tension between standard design assumptions (reaction times, visual acuity) and reality. We are seeing a spike in 'Silver Wrong Way' incidents at complex interchanges like M6 J19. It forces a re-evaluation of signage visibility and lighting standards, conflicting with energy-saving 'midnight switch-off' policies.
  • Road User Safety (Safe Systems Approach): The primary driver for pavement interventions, specifically focusing on reducing skid-related incidents and removing defects (potholes) that pose risks to motorcyclists and vehicles moving at national speed limits.
  • Zero Harm (Home Safe and Well): National Highways' strategic safety driver aiming to eliminate fatal and serious injuries on the network. This drives the requirement for rapid incident clearance to prevent secondary collisions in queuing traffic.
  • Road Investment Strategy 2 (RIS2): The Government's long-term strategy for funding and improving the Strategic Road Network from 2020 to 2025, setting the performance specification and investment plan.
  • Smart Motorways Evidence Stocktake: Strategic driver resulting from the Transport Select Committee inquiry, mandating the pause of new ALR schemes and the retrofit of Stopped Vehicle Detection (SVD) technology.

ARCHIMATE:GAP (6 components):
----------------------------
  • ADMM Data Compliance Gap: Discrepancy between the Asset Data Management Manual (ADMM) requirements and the actual GIS data delivered by construction partners. This gap prevents predictive maintenance algorithms (P50/P90 analysis) from functioning correctly on new scheme sections, creating a 'digital blind spot' for Asset Delivery mandates.
  • Asset Data Handover Gap: The operational void where Major Projects finish a scheme (e.g., A14) but the asset data hasn't fully migrated to IAM IS, leaving Maintenance teams blind to warranty status and maintenance schedules.
  • Operational Technology Integration Gap: The significant functional gap between the modern 'Digital Twin' data expected by LTC and the actual capabilities of the legacy NTIS/RCC environment.
  • BIM-to-Field Operations Gap: The disconnect between the 3D models delivered by MPD and the tools available to Traffic Officers and maintenance crews. Field operatives use iPads with simple map layers; they cannot render the heavy Revit/Navisworks models. Consequently, the expensive 'Digital Twin' often sits on a server while crews rely on PDF as-builts.
  • Operational Technology (OT) Assurance Void: A identified architectural gap. While IT systems (Exchange, SAP) are audited heavily, the bespoke OT in tunnels (e.g., Hindhead Tunnel SCADA) has limited independent assurance, relying entirely on maintenance contractors' self-certification, which poses a hidden resilience risk.
  • Rural Coverage Deficit: The operational gap between the requirement for 100% SRN communications coverage and the reality of commercial LTE/Airwave dead zones in Cumbria (A66) and the South West.

ARCHIMATE:GOAL (21 components):
-----------------------------
  • RIS2 KPI: 95% Pavement in Good Condition: The primary regulatory target monitored by the ORR. This metric creates a perverse incentive to resurface distinct sections of road to 'chase the percentage', sometimes ignoring deeper structural issues that don't manifest on the surface immediately.
Strategic performance commitment mandated by the Department for Transport (DfT) in Road Investment Strategy 2 (RIS2).
Requires National Highways to maintain at least 95% of the Strategic Road Network (SRN) pavement without need for immediate structural maintenance.
Monitored by the Office of Rail and Road (ORR).
  • Net Zero Highways 2030 (Corporate) & 2040 (Construction): The strategic mandate from DfT's Transport Decarbonisation Plan. Split into Scope 1 & 2 (Corporate - 2030) and Scope 3 (Construction - 2040). Creates significant tension with RIS3 capital efficiency targets, as low-carbon alternatives (e.g., geopolymer concrete) often carry a 15-20% cost premium over standard CEM I concrete, threatening the Benefit-Cost Ratio (BCR) of schemes like A66 and A428.
  • Sustainable Strategic Road Network Growth: The theoretical balance between economic connectivity and environmental impact. In reality, a battleground where DCO applications are delayed by carbon challenges.
  • Digital Roads Vision 2025: The strategic ambition to move to data-driven maintenance. Heavily copied from the 'Rijkswaterstaat 2030' vision. However, unlike the Dutch, NH is hampered by legacy physical assets and disjointed systems (HADDMS vs IAM-IS) that make the 'Digital Twin' concept largely theoretical currently.
The strategic ambition for a connected, automated network.
Includes V2X and Connected Corridor trials.
Currently largely aspirational; limited by the inability to get real-time latency below 500ms due to the legacy NTIS-HART stack and gaps in the fibre telecommunications network (NRTS).
  • Net Zero Highways 2030/2040/2050 Commitment: The tripartite statutory target structure: Net Zero for own operations (Scope 1 & 2) by 2030, maintenance and construction (Scope 3) by 2040, and road user emissions by 2050. Currently creating massive friction with RIS3 affordability as low-carbon solutions often carry a 'green premium' that conflicts with DfT Value for Money assessments.
  • RIS3 Enhanced Capacity Outcome: The strategic imperative to increase SRN throughput and reduce congestion cost (currently £2bn/year). Drivers for schemes like A303 Stonehenge and A46 Newark Bypass. Fundamentally at odds with environmental load restrictions.
  • Net Zero 2050 (Corporate): National Highways' existential commitment to decarbonise maintenance and construction (PAS 2080 certified). Currently failing to align with the capital delivery reality of the A66 and LTC schemes, creating an 'accountability gap' between Major Projects (RIP) and Safety, Engineering and Standards (SES).
  • Zero Harm (2040 Target): Strategic ambition to eliminate fatal and serious injuries on the SRN by 2040, currently under pressure due to statistical plateauing in RIS2 and public confidence issues regarding Smart Motorways (ALR).
  • NIS Regulations Compliance & Cyber Resilience: Strategic imperative driven by the Network and Information Systems (NIS) Regulations 2018. The current NRTS estate has air-gapped security by obscurity, but the HART architecture must introduce 'Secure by Design' principles to protect the SRN from state-sponsored cyber threats, a key requirement from the DfT and NCSC given the Critical National Infrastructure (CNI) status of the network.
  • Vision Zero Compliance (2040): The strategic goal to eliminate killed and seriously injured (KSI) casualties. This drives the 'Safe Systems' approach mandated by DfT. However, there is a massive trade-off here: introducing mixed-mode traffic (CAV + Human) initially increases risk entropy, threatening short-term KSI metrics for long-term gain.
  • Target: Zero Harm (Workforce Safety): The strategic outcome defined in 'Home Safe and Well'. It conflicts with operational reality where Traffic Officers are required to work in live lanes under 'fast roads' guidance to meet the 1-hour lane reopening target demanded by DfT.
  • Compliance with GG 104 (Safety Risk Management): The overriding DMRB standard that dictates safety communication. Public Affairs cannot over-promise safety benefits of Smart Motorways without aligning with the GG 104 safety risk assessments. This creates friction when trying to simplify complex 'hazard log' data for public consumption in 'Go Left' campaigns.
  • Efficiency (RIS2/RIS3 KPI): The binding constraint from the DfT and ORR to deliver significant operational expenditure reductions. All corporate transformation programmes (ERP, Estates, Digital) are ultimately servants to this metric. If we don't prove the savings here, the Treasury tightens the tap for RIS3 enhancements.
  • Home Safe and Well (2040): Strategic goal to eliminate fatal and serious injuries. Creates immediate trade-offs with P50 Network Availability targets when safety interventions require prolonged carriageway closures.
  • RIS Outcome: Smooth Traffic Flow: Strategic aspiration to minimize delay. In reality, this is compromised by the conflicting 'Safety' outcome which necessitates longer carriageway closures for incident investigation under DMRB GG 104.
  • Network Availability (P50) Resilience: The objective to maintain traffic flow during extreme weather. The trade-off is often managing safety (closing lanes for flooding/wind) vs. availability targets. Failure to meet P50 targets results in penalties from the ORR.
  • RIS3 Strategic Workforce Readiness: The objective to secure the capability required to deliver the Road Investment Strategy 3 (2025-2030), specifically addressing the shift from pure civil engineering to digital asset management and connected autonomous vehicle (CAV) enablement, amidst a shrinking labour market.
  • Net Zero Highways 2050: Statutory target defined in 'Net Zero Highways' plan. Broken down: Corporate Net Zero by 2030, Maintenance/Construction by 2040, Road User Emissions by 2050. The existential threat to the current RIS3 funding model.
  • RIS3 Safety Outcome: Halving Death and Serious Injury: The overriding 2025-2030 target. In reality, this is struggling against budget cuts. We are having to trade off expensive physical infrastructure changes (more Emergency Refuge Areas) against cheaper behavioral campaigns, which is a friction point with the ORR.
  • RIS2 Pavement Condition KPI Target: The strategic performance commitment monitored by the ORR to maintain at least 96.2% of the SRN pavement asset in a condition that does not require further investigation, as defined in the Road Investment Strategy 2 (RIS2).
  • Target Zero: National Highways' strategic goal to achieve zero harm on the network, driving the requirement for rigorous incident investigation and worksite protection technologies.

ARCHIMATE:LOCATION (3 components):
---------------------------------
  • QEII Bridge (Dartford Crossing): Critical strategic node on M25. High wind vulnerability. Protocol: 50mph speed limit at 50mph gusts; Closure at 60mph gusts. Economic Trade-off: Closure costs UK economy approx £1m/hour, creating immense pressure on the Severe Weather Cell to delay closure despite safety protocols.
  • A66 Trans-Pennine Route: High-altitude strategic connector. Highest risk for snow blockage. Often the first to close, severing east-west freight. Requires specific 'snow blower' asset allocation distinct from standard gritters.
  • Regional Control Centre (RCC) Godstone: Controls the south-east quadrant of the M25 and connective radials. High-pressure environment dealing with the highest traffic density in Europe. Operators here frequently override automated HART suggestions based on local tacit knowledge, creating a feedback loop gap in our automated systems data.
A key operational node managing the M25 South East quadrant.
This location is a pilot site for HART integration, exposing real-world issues where the theoretical architecture meets the reality of aging control room hardware and operator overload during severe weather events.

ARCHIMATE:OUTCOME (5 components):
--------------------------------
  • CAV Benefit: Cooperative Hazard Warning (Incident Detection): V2V/V2I latency reduction. Vehicles broadcasting 'Emergency Electronic Brake Light' (EEBL) alerts bypass the 30-60 second lag of MIDAS loop detection.
  • Restoration of Public Confidence in Smart Motorways: The primary strategic outcome sought by the 'Driving on Motorways' behavior change campaigns. This is measured against tracking polls regarding driver anxiety on ALR (All Lane Running) sections. The architecture here is split: Engineering delivers the Retrofit of Emergency Areas (ERAs), while Comms delivers the 'education' on how to use them.
  • Incident Verified and Classified: Creation of a verified incident log within the Command and Control system (HALOGEN/CHARM), categorized by severity (Bronze/Silver/Gold) and impact type (RTC, Debris, Stationary Vehicle). Triggers GG 104 safety risk assessment protocols for response dispatch.
  • RIS3 Investment Plan Delivery: Successful execution of the Road Investment Strategy (Period 3) capital portfolio, mitigating inflation risks and DCO (Development Consent Order) delays impacting schemes like the Lower Thames Crossing and A66 Northern Trans-Pennine.
  • RIS2 Safety Performance Target (50% KSI Reduction): The specific Road Investment Strategy 2 regulatory commitment to reduce Killed or Seriously Injured (KSI) casualties by 50% by 2025 compared to the 2005-2009 baseline.

ARCHIMATE:PRINCIPLE (6 components):
----------------------------------
  • DMRB GG 103 Sustainable Development: The specific Design Manual for Roads and Bridges standard mandating sustainability assessment. Unlike GG 104 (Safety Risk), which is non-negotiable, GG 103 is frequently value-engineered out during the 'Detailed Design' phase when inflation squeezes the budget. This creates a compliance gap between the 'Preliminary Design' shown to the public and the 'As-Built' reality.
The standard mandating that design and management of the SRN must support sustainable development.
It provides the technical engineering authority for enforcing environmental social value, elevating it from 'nice to have' to 'standard compliance'.
  • GG 128 Requirements for Reporting and Assurance: The specific DMRB standard translating CDM 2015 into National Highways' operational context. It forces the 'Client' role to be explicitly defined within the complex matrix of Major Projects and Operations directorates, often creating tension over who holds the budget for safety-critical design changes.
  • DMRB LD 118 Biodiversity Design: Design Manual for Roads and Bridges standard governing how biodiversity is integrated into highway design. Defines the mitigation hierarchy (Avoid, Mitigate, Compensate). Often in tension with DMRB CD 127 (Cross-sections) where engineering safety widths compete with habitat creation space.
  • DMRB GG 104 Requirements for Safety Risk Assessment: The standard defining the As Low As Reasonably Practicable (ALARP) framework. Often interpreted for structural safety, but technically applies to occupational health risks, creating friction when mitigation costs soar.
The overarching safety standard.
Mandates that any change to the highway system (including digital interventions) must demonstrate ALARP (As Low As Reasonably Practicable) risk profiles.
Often a bottleneck for agile software deployment in operational technology.
The governing safety standard.
Any introduction of L4/5 infrastructure must prove 'So Far As Is Reasonably Practicable' (SFAIRP) safety levels.
This is the primary blocker for ALR (All Lane Running) automation, as the 'Stopped Vehicle Detection' (SVD) systems (Radar) have false positive rates that complicate automated fallback strategies.
The 'Safety Risk Assessment' standard.
Often weaponized during handover: Ops rejects handover citing GG 104 non-compliance on residual risks; MP argues risks are 'As Low As Reasonably Practicable' (ALARP) to meet opening dates.
The 'Bible' for safety governance.
Every deviation from standard, including the Smart Motorway safety cases, relies on a GG 104 assessment.
The tension lies in the interpretation of 'As Low As Reasonably Practicable' (ALARP) when budgets are cut.
The 'Bible' of safety governance.
Requires As Low As Reasonably Practicable (ALARP) demonstration.
This standard is the primary constraint blocking the 'easy' rollout of digital innovations; every change to the SRN must pass a GG 104 assessment, which is time-consuming and rigorous.
It is the friction point between Safety, Engineering & Standards (SES) and Major Projects.
Every deviation from standard design (CD 123) requires a GG 104 assessment, which creates massive bottlenecks in the technical assurance process for schemes like the A303 Stonehenge.
  • Operational Design Domain (ODD) Compliance: The specific conditions under which an AV can operate. NH must guarantee infrastructure (pavement quality, signage) meets the ODD. Failure suggests NH could be liable for 'contributory negligence' if our BCI (Bridge Condition Index) or skid resistance data is inaccurate.
  • DMRB CG 501 Design of Highway Earthworks: Standard defining geotechnical design requirements. Operational tension exists where legacy earthworks (pre-standards) cannot economically meet CG 501 requirements without total reconstruction, leading to a 'manage and monitor' approach via specific maintenance requirements rather than structural resolution.

ARCHIMATE:PRODUCT (1 components):
--------------------------------
  • Personal Light Electric Vehicles (PLEVs) & Delivery Bots: The innovation class including rented e-scooters and autonomous delivery pods (Starship Technologies). Operationally, these introduce a new hazard profile: small visual footprint, rapid acceleration, and low stability. They fall outside the design parameters of legacy barrier systems (DMRB CD 377) and create specific blind-spot risks for HGV traffic on the SRN, particularly at logistics hubs near motorway junctions.

ARCHIMATE:REQUIREMENT (14 components):
------------------------------------
  • GG 104 Safety Risk Assessment: The DMRB standard for Safety Risk. Operations must demonstrate that deferring a renewal scheme (due to budget) does not increase safety risk to 'intolerable' levels. This is the primary lever used to force emergency funding release.
DMRB Standard GG 104.
Any V2X system used for control (e.g., Virtual Gantries/Variable Speed Limits) must meet safety integrity levels.
C-V2X software complexity makes proving this harder than the simpler ITS-G5 stack.
The overarching safety governance standard.
It demands that risks be reduced 'So Far As Is Reasonably Practicable' (ALARP).
Operational friction arises when retrofitting legacy assets (e.g., narrow lanes on the M6 Thelwall Viaduct) where full standard compliance is physically impossible without demolition.
  • Requirement: Implied element created to satisfy AI-discovered relationship
  • GG 104 Safety Risk Assessment (ALARP): The over-arching safety standard. If a Principal Inspection is deferred due to budget, a GG 104 assessment must prove the risk remains 'As Low As Reasonably Practicable'. This is the bureaucratic shield used by Directors when funding is tight.
  • BIM Level 3 / Information Management Mandate: The requirement for full lifecycle asset data integration. In theory, it enables predictive maintenance. In practice, legacy asset data (in systems like IAM IS) is fragmented, making it difficult to map new A428 or A66 digital assets into the brownfield maintenance regime.
  • GG 104 Safety Risk Assessment Competency: Mandatory requirement for engineers to apply GG 104 'Requirements for safety risk assessment' to AI/ML algorithms used in traffic management. Current engineering workforce lacks the mathematical grounding to apply safety arguments to non-deterministic algorithms, delaying the approval of autonomous plant machinery on the A14 legacy handover and A428 schemes.
  • Stopped Vehicle Detection (SVD) Performance Target: Specific detection rate requirement (e.g., 80% detection in <20s) for radar systems on Smart Motorways. Failure to meet this requirement constitutes a major operational risk, forcing the deployment of expensive mitigation (CCTV operators at NTOC).
  • RIS2 Performance Specification (KPIs): The outcome contract with government. Includes strict metrics like 'P50 Network Availability' and 'LARS' (Lane Availability Reporting System). Major Projects often negatively impact LARS during construction (roadworks), creating tension between the Capital Delivery (building it) and Operations (running it) directorates regarding road space booking.
  • PAS 2080:2023 Certification: Global standard for managing carbon in infrastructure. National Highways is certified, but cascading this requirement to Tier 1 and Tier 2 supply chain (Balfour Beatty, Costain, etc.) is the operational bottleneck.
  • Net Zero Highways (Construction & Maintenance) 2040: The corporate requirement to decarbonize maintenance activities, mandating the use of warm mix asphalts, in-situ recycling, and low-carbon materials in pavement renewals.
  • Maximize Lane Availability (KPI): The RIS2/RIS3 performance metric requirement to ensure the SRN is open to traffic. Incident clearance must be efficient to minimize 'Lane Impact' duration, impacting the KPI for Network Availability.
  • Net Zero Carbon 2050: Strategic requirement compelling CIP schemes to minimize embodied carbon in construction (PAS 2080) and support low-carbon network operations.
  • RIS2 KPI: Network Availability: Performance metric requiring >98% of the SRN to be available to traffic, penalizing unauthorized or overrunning roadworks.
  • Home Safe and Well Strategy: National Highways' health, safety, and wellbeing strategy aiming to eliminate fatal and serious risks on the SRN by 2040.
  • KSI Reduction Target (50% by 2025): Performance metric mandated by the DfT and ORR to reduce Killed or Seriously Injured numbers by 50% against the 2005-2009 baseline, driving safety interventions.

ARCHIMATE:RESOURCE (3 components):
---------------------------------
  • Electric Traffic Officer Fleet (PHEV/BEV): Transition of the Land Rover Discovery and Volvo operational fleet to PHEV/BEV. Operational tension: Range anxiety during major incidents (e.g., 6+ hour closures on M6) and lack of rapid charging at outstation depots like Heston or Perry Barr.
  • University Partnership (UWE/Coventry): Talent pipeline mechanism intended to funnel graduates with ITS (Intelligent Transport Systems) degrees directly into Safety, Engineering and Standards (SES) directorate. Success is currently limited by the location of NH offices (Birmingham/Guildford) vs tech hubs.
  • Operational Technology (OT) Engineering Workforce: Specialised engineers required to bridge the gap between legacy roadside equipment (MIDAS, signals) and modern IP-based networks. Critical for maintaining the National Traffic Operations Centre (NTOC) and implementing the Smart Motorways Evidence Stocktake recommendations.

ARCHIMATE:STAKEHOLDER (20 components):
------------------------------------
  • Transport Focus: Represents the road user. They input into Route Strategies via the Strategic Roads User Survey (SRUS). Their data often highlights 'potholes' and 'roadworks management' as top priorities, which conflicts with the internal engineering focus on major capacity enhancements. They force the inclusion of 'User Satisfaction' metrics into the RIS performance specification.
User watchdog representing the driver's voice.
Heavily critical of roadworks communication accuracy and the 'feel' of safety on Smart Motorways, driving the shift in KPI metrics towards 'User Confidence' rather than just 'Statistical Safety'.
  • Office of Rail and Road (ORR): The independent monitor that holds NH's feet to the fire regarding efficiency (KPIs). They scrutinize the Route Strategy evidence base to ensure proposed schemes actually deliver value (BCR). They specifically challenge the 'Efficiency' KPI within the Route Strategies, questioning if the stated benefits of schemes like the Lower Thames Crossing justify the capital expenditure.
The regulator.
They don't care about our 'Digital Vision' if it compromises safety.
Following the Transport Committee report, they are aggressively auditing our technology performance (SVD availability, MIDAS reliability).
They hold the licence to fine us if we miss RIS2 KPI targets like Network Availability.
The independent regulator monitoring National Highways' performance against RIS2 KPI targets.
They specifically scrutinize the 'User Satisfaction' KPI (target 82%) and 'Network Availability' (P50).
Communications teams manage the narrative around the Annual Assessment of Safety and Performance, often trying to contextualize 'Red' or 'Amber' performance indicators.
The Highways Monitor.
They are currently scrutinizing NH's failure to meet KPI targets (specifically KP1 on safety and Availability).
Their 'Annual Assessment' drives the urgency of internal audit activities.
The independent regulator holding NH's feet to the fire on KPI compliance.
They specifically intervene on 'Regionally Significant' safety metrics and forced the 2022 Smart Motorways Evidence Stocktake.
Their annual assessment drives the frantic end-of-year maintenance rushes to meet availability targets.
Statutory monitor enforcing the RIS Performance Specification.
They focus heavily on 'Efficiency' and 'Safety' metrics, often creating tension with NH Operations who prioritize real-time network flow over retrospective efficiency reporting.
  • Host Local Authorities (Planning & Highways): County and Unitary councils (e.g., Kent County Council, Thurrock Council) who hold statutory power to comment on the adequacy of consultation. Relationship is often adversarial due to tensions between national strategic needs (SRN capacity) and local network impacts (rat-running, air quality).
  • SRN Road User: The end-user of the Strategic Road Network. Currently characterized by high anxiety regarding automation (ADAS) and 'Smart' infrastructure. Their behavior is the primary uncontrolled variable in our safety models (GG 104). Trust is currently measured via the SRN User Survey, where perception of safety often lags behind statistical safety (STATS19 data).
  • Logistics UK & Major Freight Hauliers: Key users demanding predictable journey times over pure speed. They are pushing for the 'Infrastructure as a Service' model where they pay premiums for guaranteed slots or platooning privileges on the M6 and M1. Their requirements clash with the 'open access' principle of the public highway.
  • Vulnerable Road Users (VRU) & Disabled Motorists: Includes the 2.4m Blue Badge holders. Critical focus following the Smart Motorways Stocktake. The fear of breaking down in a Live Lane (ALR) without a hard shoulder is significantly higher in this demographic, driving political pressure from the Transport Select Committee.
  • SRN Road User (Freight & Personal): The ultimate consumer of the network. Experience is currently fragmented between digital journey planning (Google/Waze), physical infrastructure reality (potholes/roadworks), and incident response. Highly sensitive to journey time reliability (P50) and information latency.
  • Safety, Engineering & Standards Directorate: Corporate safety authority defining standards and engineering interventions
  • Operations Directorate: Runs the SRN and manages real-time safety risks

  • Digital Services Directorate: Provides systems, platforms and communications infrastructure

  • Major Projects Directorate: Designs and delivers capital road investment schemes

Leads long-term network strategy and investment planning

  • Environment & Sustainability Directorate: Leads carbon, biodiversity and environmental compliance

Oversees budgets, reporting and compliance

ARCHIMATE:VALUESTREAM (27 components):
------------------------------------
  • Incident Management (Detection to Restoration): The operational value chain for SRN incident handling, governed by the 10-minute response KPI (RIS2) and dragged down by legacy C&C systems. It starts with source detection (SVD, MIDAS, or eCall) and ends with P50 availability restoration.
The operational heartbeat of the SRN.
Critical tension exists here between 'Road Availability' (KPI P50) and 'Safety' (DMRB GG 104).
Currently stressed by the transition from legacy inductive loops to radar-based detection on ALR (All Lane Running) schemes like M6 J13-15.
  • Incident Clearance and Carriageway Restoration: The critical operational phase of incident management targeting the RIS2 KPI of clearing 86% of incidents within 1 hour. This stream represents the high-friction period between 'Scene Controlled' and 'All Lanes Running', often characterized by conflict between Police forensic requirements (statutory investigation) and National Highways' network availability mandates.
  • Keep Network Safe: Reduce fatalities and serious injuries on the SRN
Reduce fatalities and injuries across the SRN
The primary operational value stream for National Highways, aligned with the 'Home Safe and Well' strategy and RIS2/RIS3 safety performance key performance indicators (KPIs) such as KSIs (Killed or Seriously Injured).
It encompasses incident prevention, real-time management, and infrastructure resilience.
  • Keep Network Available: Ensure roads remain open and flowing reliably
Ensure roads remain open and traffic flows reliably
The end-to-end value stream responsible for maximizing Safe Lane Availability on the Strategic Road Network (SRN).
It encompasses the monitoring of flow, management of unplanned incidents, and coordination of planned works to meet RIS2 KPI targets (typically >98% availability).
  • Maintain Strategic Road Network (SRN): Sustain infrastructure condition and reliability
Sustain infrastructure condition and lifecycle performance
The end-to-end value stream for ensuring the Strategic Road Network (SRN) remains safe, serviceable, and compliant with statutory duties under the Highways Act 1980.
Encompasses the lifecycle from defect detection to renewal, aligned with RIS2/RIS3 performance KPIs such as Pavement Condition (KPI) and Network Availability.
The end-to-end value stream for preserving the asset value and safety of the SRN, encompassing pavement, structures, and technology assets in alignment with RIS2/RIS3 performance specifications and DMRB standards.
  • Enhance Network: Improve capacity, resilience and connectivity
The end-to-end value stream for upgrading the Strategic Road Network (SRN) through the delivery of Road Investment Strategy (RIS2/RIS3) commitments.
This includes the planning, design, construction, and operational handover of Nationally Significant Infrastructure Projects (NSIPs) such as the Lower Thames Crossing and the A66 Northern Trans-Pennine, as well as Regional Investment Programme (RIP) schemes.
Improve capacity and resilience through schemes
The end-to-end value stream for delivering Road Investment Strategy (RIS2/RIS3) capital enhancements, including Nationally Significant Infrastructure Projects (NSIPs) and Regional Investment Programmes (RIP).
Covers the lifecycle from strategy to handover.
  • Enable Customer Journeys: Provide reliable and understandable journeys
Provide reliable information and journey confidence
The primary operational value stream for National Highways, encompassing the planning, operation, and maintenance of the Strategic Road Network (SRN) to ensure safe, reliable, and efficient travel.
This stream aligns with RIS2/RIS3 performance outcomes regarding safety (halving death/injury) and service quality (p50 journey time reliability).

  • Safety Assurance: The end-to-end value stream for ensuring safety across the Strategic Road Network (SRN), aligned with the 'Home Safe and Well' strategy and 'Target Zero' 2040 ambitions. Encompasses policy setting, design assurance (RSA), construction safety, and operational monitoring.
Reduce risk to workers and road users

  • Scheme Design Safety Assurance: Application of safety standards to RIS2/RIS3 projects (e.g., A66 Northern Trans-Pennine, Lower Thames Crossing). Includes Road Safety Audits (RSA) per GG 119 and WCHAR assessments per GG 142.
  • Operational Safety Monitoring: Continuous monitoring of SRN performance using STATS19 data, POPE (Post Opening Project Evaluation), and Smart Motorway Stocktake evidence analysis to identify high-risk locations (Cluster Sites).

ARCHIMATE:VALUESTREAMSTAGE (20 components):
-----------------------------------------
  • Incident Detection & Initial Assessment Stage: Critical initial phase of the Incident Management Value Stream (IMVS). Encompasses the T0 (Time of Occurrence) to T1 (Time of Verification) window. Heavily scrutinized under ORR safety metrics for All Lane Running (ALR) schemes. Involves ingesting telemetry from MIDAS, SVD Radar, and eCall data into the Control Room environment.
The initial operational phase T0 to T1 within the Incident Management lifecycle.
Encompasses the receipt of alerts via MIDAS, SVD (Stopped Vehicle Detection), or ERT, and the initial verification by ROC operators.
Critical for meeting '60-minute clearance' KPIs and reducing secondary collision risks on Smart Motorways.
  • Monitor Network Status: The continuous operational phase involving the ingestion of real-time data from MIDAS loops, CCTV, and Floating Car Data (FCD) to establish the current state of traffic flow and asset availability across the SRN.
Continuous 24/7 surveillance of the SRN using roadside technology (MIDAS, CCTV) and data feeds to detect anomalies, congestion, or flow breakdown in real-time.
  • Manage Incidents and Congestion: The reactive and proactive intervention stage where Regional Control Centres (RCCs) and the National Traffic Operations Centre (NTOC) utilise signaling and Traffic Officer deployment to mitigate disruption, adhering to command and control protocols.
  • Inform Road Users: The dissemination of actionable intelligence to customers via Variable Message Signs (VMS), digital channels, and third-party service providers (ISPs) to influence driver behavior and route choice.
  • Identify Asset Deterioration: The stage involving data acquisition through TRACS surveys, visual inspections (GVI/CVI), and remote monitoring to detect defects against GM 701 and CS 450 standards.
  • Plan Maintenance Interventions: Prioritisation of schemes based on value management (VfM), safety risk (GG 104), and funding availability within the RIS capital delivery programme.
  • Secure Network Occupancy: Booking road space for works to minimise journey time reliability impact, coordinating with NMW (Network Management Weekly) and Schedule of Road Works (SRW).
  • Execute Renewal Works: Physical delivery of maintenance (resurfacing, barrier repair, technology replacement) by supply chain partners (Scheme Delivery Framework) under CD 123 and relevant MCHW standards.
  • Manage Network Disruptions: The operational response to unplanned events (RTCs, debris, spills) to restore capacity. This aligns with the 'Clear' phase of the CLEAR initiative (Collision, Lead, Evaluate, Act, Re-open).
  • Plan Network Occupancy: Strategic booking of road space for maintenance and improvement schemes (RIS delivery) to minimize customer impact, avoiding conflict between concurrent delivery partners.
  • Plan Network Usage: The stage where road users (freight and public) utilize National Highways data services to plan routes, accounting for roadworks (NRSWA), congestion, and weather events.
  • Traverse Strategic Road Network: The core operational phase where users physically travel on the SRN, supported by passive engineering (pavement quality) and active traffic management (Smart Motorways, VMS).
  • Resolve Journey Disruptions: The responsive stage activated during unplanned events (collisions, debris, breakdown), involving Traffic Officer intervention and ROC coordination to restore network availability (P50/P90 metrics).
  • Mobilise Supply Chain Partners: The stage of onboarding contractors for schemes like A66 Northern Trans-Pennine or Smart Motorway retrofits. Includes verifying Highways Common Induction (HCI), establishing Project Bank Accounts (PBA), and configuring contract management systems (CEMAR).
  • Manage Contract Performance: Ongoing administration of NEC4 ECC contracts during delivery. Includes managing Early Warning Notices (EWN), Compensation Events (CE), and tracking performance against the Motivating Success Toolkit (MST) or Collaborative Performance Framework (CPF).
  • Verify Technical Compliance: Assurance that delivered works meet DMRB standards (e.g., GG 101, CD 123). Involves reviewing design packages, managing Departures from Standards (DfS), and conducting site assurance visits.
  • Develop Strategic Investment Plan: The phase involving Route Strategies analysis to identify network needs, consultation with Transport Focus, and negotiation with the DfT to define the Road Investment Strategy (RIS) portfolio.
  • Develop and Design Schemes: Progressing schemes through the early stages of the Project Control Framework (PCF Stages 0-3), including options selection, preliminary design, and securing Development Consent Orders (DCO) for NSIPs.
  • Construct and Deliver Infrastructure: Physical delivery of major projects (e.g., A66 Northern Trans-Pennine, Lower Thames Crossing) and renewal programmes, managed via Regional Delivery Partnerships (RDP) and adhering to CDM 2015 regulations.
  • Handover to Operations: PCF Stage 7 activities involving the transfer of new assets to the Operations Directorate, updating IAM IS (Asset Management System), and ensuring road space is returned to traffic (Open for Traffic).
